	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Greeks</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">

		<div id="contentSub"></div>

		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the ethnic group known as the Greeks and their descendants worldwide. For information on the population of Greece, see <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Greece" title="Demographics of Greece">Demographics of Greece</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Greeks_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Greeks (disambiguation)">Greeks (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color:#b0c4de;">Languages</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color:#b0c4de;">Religion</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">Primarily <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church" title="Greek Orthodox Church">Greek Orthodox Church</a></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;padding-top:0.5em;font-size:85%;text-align:left;"><hr /><sup><small>a</small></sup> Citizens of Greece and the Republic of Cyprus. The Greek government does not collect information about ethnic self-determination at the national censuses.<br /><sup><small>b</small></sup> Includes those of ancestral descent.<br /><sup><small>c</small></sup> Those whose stated ethnic origins included "Greek" among others. The number of those whose stated ethnic origin is <i>solely</i> "Greek" is 145,250. An additional 3,395 Cypriots of undeclared ethnicity live in Canada.<br /><sup><small>d</small></sup>Approx. 60,000 <a href="/wiki/Griko_people" title="Griko people">Griko people</a> and 30,000 post WW2 migrants.<br /><sup><small>e</small></sup> "Including descendants".<br /><sup><small>g</small></sup>Includes people with "cultural roots".</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Greeks</b> or <b>Hellenes</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;h&#39; in &#39;hi&#39;">h</span><span title="/ɛ/: &#39;e&#39; in &#39;dress&#39;">ɛ</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/iː/: &#39;ee&#39; in &#39;fleece&#39;">iː</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="&#39;z&#39; in &#39;zoom&#39;">z</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">Έλληνες</span>, <i>Éllines</i> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Greek" title="Help:IPA/Greek">[ˈelines]</a></span>) are an <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_group" title="Ethnic group">ethnic group</a> native to <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_Cypriots" title="Greek Cypriots">Cyprus</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Albania" title="Greeks in Albania">Albania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Italy" title="Greeks in Italy">Italy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Turkey#History" title="Greeks in Turkey">Turkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Egypt" title="Greeks in Egypt">Egypt</a> and, to a lesser extent, other countries surrounding the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>. They also form a significant <a href="/wiki/Greek_diaspora" title="Greek diaspora">diaspora</a>, with Greek communities established around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-Roberts1_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts1-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Greek colonies and communities have been historically established on the shores of the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>, but the Greek people have always been centered on the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ionian_Sea" title="Ionian Sea">Ionian</a> seas, where the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek language</a> has been spoken since the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sutton_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sutton-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Until the early 20th century, Greeks were distributed between the <a href="/wiki/Greek_peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek peninsula">Greek peninsula</a>, the western coast of <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>, the Black Sea coast, <a href="/wiki/Cappadocia" title="Cappadocia">Cappadocia</a> in central Anatolia, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>, Cyprus, and <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sutton_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sutton-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Many of these regions coincided to a large extent with the borders of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> of the late 11th century and the Eastern Mediterranean areas of ancient <a href="/wiki/Greek_colonies" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek colonies">Greek colonization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The cultural centers of the Greeks have included <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thessalonica" class="mw-redirect" title="Thessalonica">Thessalonica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smyrna" title="Smyrna">Smyrna</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> at various periods.
</p><p>In recent times, most ethnic Greeks live within the borders of the modern Greek state and Cyprus. The <a href="/wiki/Greek_genocide" title="Greek genocide">Greek genocide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Population_exchange_between_Greece_and_Turkey" title="Population exchange between Greece and Turkey">population exchange between Greece and Turkey</a> nearly ended the three millennia-old Greek presence in Asia Minor. Other longstanding Greek populations can be found from southern Italy to the Caucasus and southern <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Russia" class="mw-redirect" title="Greeks in Russia">Russia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Ukraine" title="Greeks in Ukraine">Ukraine</a> and in the <a href="/wiki/Greek_diaspora" title="Greek diaspora">Greek diaspora</a> communities in a number of other countries. Today, most Greeks are officially registered as members of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church" title="Greek Orthodox Church">Greek Orthodox Church</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Throughout history, Greeks have greatly influenced and contributed to <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">culture</a>, visual arts, exploration, theatre, <a href="/wiki/Literature" title="Literature">literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Politics" title="Politics">politics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Architecture" title="Architecture">architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Music" title="Music">music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, medicine, <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>, technology, commerce, cuisine and sports.
</p>

</div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Greece" title="History of Greece">History of Greece</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Proto_Greek_Area_reconstruction.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Proto_Greek_Area_reconstruction.png/240px-Proto_Greek_Area_reconstruction.png" decoding="async" width="240" height="198" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Proto_Greek_Area_reconstruction.png/360px-Proto_Greek_Area_reconstruction.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fd/Proto_Greek_Area_reconstruction.png 2x" data-file-width="460" data-file-height="380" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Proto_Greek_Area_reconstruction.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A reconstruction of the 3rd millennium BC "Proto-Greek area", by <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_I._Georgiev" title="Vladimir I. Georgiev">Vladimir I. Georgiev</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gold_death-mask,_known_as_the_%27mask_of_Agamemnon%27,_from_Mycenae,_grave_Circle_A,_16th_century_BC,_Athens_Archaeological_Museum,_Greece_(22669073522).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" decoding="async" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Gold_death-mask%2C_known_as_the_%27mask_of_Agamemnon%27%2C_from_Mycenae%2C_grave_Circle_A%2C_16th_century_BC%2C_Athens_Archaeological_Museum%2C_Greece_%2822669073522%29.jpg/330px-Gold_death-mask%2C_known_as_the_%27mask_of_Agamemnon%27%2C_from_Mycenae%2C_grave_Circle_A%2C_16th_century_BC%2C_Athens_Archaeological_Museum%2C_Greece_%2822669073522%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3875" data-file-height="2950" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gold_death-mask,_known_as_the_%27mask_of_Agamemnon%27,_from_Mycenae,_grave_Circle_A,_16th_century_BC,_Athens_Archaeological_Museum,_Greece_(22669073522).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greece" title="Mycenaean Greece">Mycenaean</a> <a href="/wiki/Death_mask" title="Death mask">funeral mask</a> known as "<a href="/wiki/Mask_of_Agamemnon" title="Mask of Agamemnon">Mask of Agamemnon</a>", 16th c. BC</div></div></div>
<p>The Greeks speak the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek language</a>, which forms its own unique branch within the <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> family of languages, the <a href="/wiki/Hellenic_languages" title="Hellenic languages">Hellenic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sutton_40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sutton-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> They are part of a group of classical ethnicities, described by <a href="/wiki/Anthony_D._Smith" title="Anthony D. Smith">Anthony D. Smith</a> as an "archetypal diaspora people".<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Proto-Greek_language" title="Proto-Greek language">Proto-Greek language</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Ancient_Greek_tribes" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Ancient Greek tribes">List of Ancient Greek tribes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_religion" title="Ancient Greek religion">Ancient Greek religion</a></div>
<p>The Proto-Greeks probably arrived at the area now called Greece, in the southern tip of the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkan peninsula</a>, at the end of the 3rd millennium BC.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup> The sequence of migrations into the Greek mainland during the <a href="/wiki/2nd_millennium_BC" title="2nd millennium BC">2nd millennium BC</a> has to be reconstructed on the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_dialects" title="Ancient Greek dialects">ancient Greek dialects</a>, as they presented themselves centuries later and are therefore subject to some uncertainties. There were at least two migrations, the first being the <a href="/wiki/Ionians" title="Ionians">Ionians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aeolians" title="Aeolians">Aeolians</a>, which resulted in <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greece" title="Mycenaean Greece">Mycenaean Greece</a> by the 16th century BC,<sup id="cite_ref-Brit1_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brit1-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> and the second, the <a href="/wiki/Dorian_invasion" title="Dorian invasion">Dorian invasion</a>, around the 11th century BC, displacing the <a href="/wiki/Arcadocypriot_Greek" title="Arcadocypriot Greek">Arcadocypriot dialects</a>, which descended from the Mycenaean period. Both migrations occur at incisive periods, the Mycenaean at the transition to the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Late Bronze Age</a> and the Doric at the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_collapse" class="mw-redirect" title="Bronze Age collapse">Bronze Age collapse</a>.
</p><p>An alternative hypothesis has been put forth by linguist <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_I._Georgiev" title="Vladimir I. Georgiev">Vladimir Georgiev</a>, who places Proto-Greek speakers in northwestern Greece by the <a href="/wiki/Early_Helladic" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Helladic">Early Helladic</a> period (3rd millennium BC), i.e. towards the end of the <a href="/wiki/European_Neolithic" class="mw-redirect" title="European Neolithic">European Neolithic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Linguists <a href="/wiki/Russell_Gray" title="Russell Gray">Russell Gray</a> and Quentin Atkinson in a 2003 paper using computational methods on <a href="/wiki/Swadesh_list" title="Swadesh list">Swadesh lists</a> have arrived at a somewhat earlier estimate, around 5000 BC for Greco-Armenian split and the emergence of Greek as a separate linguistic lineage around 4000 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mycenaean">Mycenaean</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greece" title="Mycenaean Greece">Mycenaean Greece</a></div>
<p>In <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1600 BC, the Mycenaean Greeks borrowed from the <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">Minoan civilization</a> its syllabic writing system (<a href="/wiki/Linear_A" title="Linear A">Linear A</a>) and developed their own <a href="/wiki/Syllabic_script" class="mw-redirect" title="Syllabic script">syllabic script</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Linear_B" title="Linear B">Linear B</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> providing the first and oldest written evidence of <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> The Mycenaeans quickly penetrated the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean Sea</a> and, by the 15th century BC, had reached <a href="/wiki/Rhodes" title="Rhodes">Rhodes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> and the shores of <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Asia Minor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sutton_40-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sutton-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Around 1200 BC, the <a href="/wiki/Dorians" title="Dorians">Dorians</a>, another Greek-speaking people, followed from <a href="/wiki/Epirus" title="Epirus">Epirus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Traditionally, historians have believed that the <a href="/wiki/Dorian_invasion" title="Dorian invasion">Dorian invasion</a> caused the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_civilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Mycenaean civilization">Mycenaean civilization</a>, but it is likely the main attack was made by seafaring raiders (<a href="/wiki/Sea_Peoples" title="Sea Peoples">Sea Peoples</a>) who sailed into the eastern Mediterranean around 1180 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Dorian_invasion" title="Dorian invasion">Dorian invasion</a> was followed by a poorly attested period of migrations, appropriately called the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Dark_Ages" title="Greek Dark Ages">Greek Dark Ages</a>, but by 800 BC the landscape of <a href="/wiki/Archaic_Greece" title="Archaic Greece">Archaic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">Classical Greece</a> was discernible.<sup id="cite_ref-Castleden2_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Castleden2-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Greeks of classical antiquity idealized their Mycenaean ancestors and the Mycenaean period as a glorious era of heroes, closeness of the gods and material wealth.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homeric Epics</a> (i.e. <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Odyssey" title="Odyssey">Odyssey</a></i>) were especially and generally accepted as part of the Greek past and it was not until the time of <a href="/wiki/Euhemerism" title="Euhemerism">Euhemerism</a> that scholars began to question Homer's historicity.<sup id="cite_ref-Castleden2_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Castleden2-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> As part of the Mycenaean heritage that survived, the names of the gods and goddesses of Mycenaean Greece (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poseidon" title="Poseidon">Poseidon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hades" title="Hades">Hades</a>) became major figures of the <a href="/wiki/Twelve_Olympians" title="Twelve Olympians">Olympian Pantheon</a> of later antiquity.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical">Classical</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">Classical Greece</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r923042769/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti class="tsingle" style="width:102px;max-width:102px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Francesco_Hayez_001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Francesco_Hayez_001.jpg/100px-Francesco_Hayez_001.jpg" decoding="async" width="100" height="129" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Francesco_Hayez_001.jpg/150px-Francesco_Hayez_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Francesco_Hayez_001.jpg/200px-Francesco_Hayez_001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2024" data-file-height="2604" /></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="thumbcaption" style="background-color:transparent">The three great philosophers of the classical era; <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a></div></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ethnogenesis" title="Ethnogenesis">ethnogenesis</a> of the Greek nation is linked to the development of Pan-Hellenism in the 8th century BC.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> According to some scholars, the foundational event was the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Olympic_Games" title="Ancient Olympic Games">Olympic Games</a> in 776 BC, when the idea of a common Hellenism among the Greek tribes was first translated into a shared cultural experience and Hellenism was primarily a matter of common culture.<sup id="cite_ref-Roberts1_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts1-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The works of <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a> (i.e. <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Odyssey" title="Odyssey">Odyssey</a></i>) and <a href="/wiki/Hesiod" title="Hesiod">Hesiod</a> (i.e. <i><a href="/wiki/Theogony" title="Theogony">Theogony</a></i>) were written in the 8th century BC, becoming the basis of the national religion, ethos, history and mythology.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Pythia" title="Pythia">Oracle of Apollo at Delphi</a> was established in this period.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical period</a> of Greek civilization covers a time spanning from the early 5th century BC to the <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Death of Alexander the Great">death of Alexander the Great</a>, in 323 BC (some authors prefer to split this period into "Classical", from the end of the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Persian_Wars" title="Greco-Persian Wars">Greco-Persian Wars</a> to the end of the Peloponnesian War, and "Fourth Century", up to the death of Alexander). It is so named because it set the standards by which Greek civilization would be judged in later eras.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The Classical period is also described as the "Golden Age" of Greek civilization, and its art, philosophy, architecture and literature would be instrumental in the formation and development of Western culture.
</p><p>While the Greeks of the classical era understood themselves to belong to a common Hellenic <a href="/wiki/Genos" title="Genos">genos</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> their first loyalty was to their city and they saw nothing incongruous about warring, often brutally, with other Greek <a href="/wiki/Polis" title="Polis">city-states</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Peloponnesian_War" title="Peloponnesian War">Peloponnesian War</a>, the large scale civil war between the two most powerful Greek city-states <a href="/wiki/Classical_Athens" title="Classical Athens">Athens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Delian_League" title="Delian League">their</a> <a href="/wiki/Peloponnesian_League" title="Peloponnesian League">allies</a>, left both greatly weakened.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_the_Great_mosaic.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Alexander_the_Great_mosaic.jpg/220px-Alexander_the_Great_mosaic.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Alexander_the_Great_mosaic.jpg/330px-Alexander_the_Great_mosaic.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Alexander_the_Great_mosaic.jpg/440px-Alexander_the_Great_mosaic.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_the_Great_mosaic.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>, whose conquests led to the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Hellenistic Age">Hellenistic Age</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Most of the feuding Greek city-states were, in some scholars' opinions, united by force under the banner of <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip</a>'s and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>'s Pan-Hellenic ideals, though others might generally opt, rather, for an explanation of "<a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)" title="Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">Macedonian</a> conquest for the sake of conquest" or at least conquest for the sake of riches, glory and power and view the "ideal" as useful propaganda directed towards the city-states.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In any case, Alexander's toppling of the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a>, after his victories at the battles of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Granicus" title="Battle of the Granicus">Granicus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Issus" title="Battle of Issus">Issus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gaugamela" title="Battle of Gaugamela">Gaugamela</a>, and his advance as far as modern-day <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> provided an important outlet for Greek culture, via the creation of colonies and trade routes along the way.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> While the Alexandrian empire did not survive its creator's death intact, the cultural implications of the spread of Hellenism across much of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> were to prove long lived as Greek became the <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i>, a position it retained even in <a href="/wiki/Roman_era" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman era">Roman times</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Many Greeks settled in <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_Greece" title="Hellenistic Greece">Hellenistic</a> cities like <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seleucia" title="Seleucia">Seleucia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Two thousand years later, there are still communities in <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, like the <a href="/wiki/Kalash_people" title="Kalash people">Kalash</a>, who claim to be descended from Greek settlers.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hellenistic">Hellenistic</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic period</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_Greece" title="Hellenistic Greece">Hellenistic Greece</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Diadochen1.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Diadochen1.png/250px-Diadochen1.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="120" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Diadochen1.png/375px-Diadochen1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Diadochen1.png/500px-Diadochen1.png 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="961" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Diadochen1.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Hellenistic realms c. 300 BC as divided by the <a href="/wiki/Diadochi" title="Diadochi">Diadochi</a>; the Μacedonian Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Cassander" title="Cassander">Cassander</a> (green), the <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Kingdom" title="Ptolemaic Kingdom">Ptolemaic Kingdom</a> (dark blue), the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a> (yellow), the areas controlled by <a href="/wiki/Lysimachus" title="Lysimachus">Lysimachus</a> (orange) and <a href="/wiki/Epirus" title="Epirus">Epirus</a> (red)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bust_of_Cleopatra_VII_-_Altes_Museum_-_Berlin_-_Germany_2017_(3).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Bust_of_Cleopatra_VII_-_Altes_Museum_-_Berlin_-_Germany_2017_%283%29.jpg/175px-Bust_of_Cleopatra_VII_-_Altes_Museum_-_Berlin_-_Germany_2017_%283%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="175" height="263" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Bust_of_Cleopatra_VII_-_Altes_Museum_-_Berlin_-_Germany_2017_%283%29.jpg/263px-Bust_of_Cleopatra_VII_-_Altes_Museum_-_Berlin_-_Germany_2017_%283%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Bust_of_Cleopatra_VII_-_Altes_Museum_-_Berlin_-_Germany_2017_%283%29.jpg/350px-Bust_of_Cleopatra_VII_-_Altes_Museum_-_Berlin_-_Germany_2017_%283%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="5184" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bust_of_Cleopatra_VII_-_Altes_Museum_-_Berlin_-_Germany_2017_(3).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bust of <a href="/wiki/Cleopatra_VII" class="mw-redirect" title="Cleopatra VII">Cleopatra VII</a> (<a href="/wiki/Altes_Museum" title="Altes Museum">Altes Museum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a>), the last ruler of a Hellenistic Kingdom (apart the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Greek_Kingdom" title="Indo-Greek Kingdom">Indo-Greek Kingdom</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_civilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Hellenistic civilization">Hellenistic civilization</a> was the next period of Greek civilization, the beginnings of which are usually placed at Alexander's death.<sup id="cite_ref-Boardman364_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boardman364-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic age</a>, so called because it saw the partial <a href="/wiki/Hellenization" title="Hellenization">Hellenization</a> of many non-Greek cultures,<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> lasted until the conquest of <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Ptolemaic Egypt">Egypt</a> by Rome in 30 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-Boardman364_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boardman364-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This age saw the Greeks move towards larger cities and a reduction in the importance of the city-state. These larger cities were parts of the still larger <a href="/wiki/Diadochi" title="Diadochi">Kingdoms of the Diadochi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BritHel_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BritHel-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Greeks, however, remained aware of their past, chiefly through the study of the works of Homer and the classical authors.<sup id="cite_ref-Harris_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harris-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> An important factor in maintaining Greek identity was contact with <i><a href="/wiki/Barbarian" title="Barbarian">barbarian</a></i> (non-Greek) peoples, which was deepened in the new cosmopolitan environment of the multi-ethnic Hellenistic kingdoms.<sup id="cite_ref-Harris_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harris-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> This led to a strong desire among Greeks to organize the transmission of the Hellenic <i><a href="/wiki/Paideia" title="Paideia">paideia</a></i> to the next generation.<sup id="cite_ref-Harris_81-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harris-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Greek science, technology and mathematics are generally considered to have reached their peak during the Hellenistic period.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Greeks" class="mw-redirect" title="Indo-Greeks">Indo-Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian_Kingdom" title="Greco-Bactrian Kingdom">Greco-Bactrian</a> kingdoms, <a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhism" title="Greco-Buddhism">Greco-Buddhism</a> was spreading and Greek missionaries would play an important role in propagating it to <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Further east, the Greeks of <a href="/wiki/Alexandria_Eschate" title="Alexandria Eschate">Alexandria Eschate</a> became known to the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_people" title="Chinese people">Chinese people</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Dayuan" title="Dayuan">Dayuan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_Empire">Roman Empire</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Roman_Greece" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Greece">Roman Greece</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_world" title="Greco-Roman world">Greco-Roman world</a></div>
<p>Between 168 BC and 30 BC, the entire Greek world was conquered by Rome, and almost all of the world's Greek speakers lived as citizens or subjects of the Roman Empire. Despite their military superiority, the Romans admired and became <a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_world" title="Greco-Roman world">heavily influenced</a> by the achievements of Greek culture, hence <a href="/wiki/Horace" title="Horace">Horace</a>'s famous statement: <i>Graecia capta ferum victorem cepit</i> ("Greece, although captured, took its wild conqueror captive").<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> In the centuries following the Roman conquest of the Greek world, the Greek and Roman cultures merged into a single <a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman" class="mw-redirect" title="Greco-Roman">Greco-Roman</a> culture.
</p><p>In the religious sphere, this was a period of profound change. The spiritual revolution that took place, saw a waning of the old Greek religion, whose decline beginning in the 3rd century BC continued with the introduction of new religious movements from the East.<sup id="cite_ref-Roberts1_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts1-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The cults of deities like <a href="/wiki/Isis" title="Isis">Isis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mithra" title="Mithra">Mithra</a> were introduced into the Greek world.<sup id="cite_ref-BritHel_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BritHel-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BritHelRel_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BritHelRel-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Greek-speaking communities of the Hellenized East were instrumental in the spread of early Christianity in the 2nd and 3rd centuries,<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> and Christianity's early leaders and writers (notably <a href="/wiki/Saint_Paul" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Paul">Saint Paul</a>) were generally Greek-speaking,<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> though none were from Greece. However, Greece itself had a tendency to cling to paganism and was not one of the influential centers of early Christianity: in fact, some ancient Greek religious practices remained in vogue until the end of the 4th century,<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> with some areas such as the southeastern Peloponnese remaining pagan until well into the 10th century AD.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> The region of <a href="/wiki/Tsakonia" title="Tsakonia">Tsakonia</a> remained pagan until the ninth century and as such its inhabitants were  referred to as <i>Hellenes</i>, in the sense of being pagan, by their Christianized Greek brethren in mainstream Byzantine society.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While ethnic distinctions still existed in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, they became secondary to religious considerations and the renewed empire used Christianity as a tool to support its cohesion and promoted a robust Roman national identity.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> From the early centuries of the <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">Common Era</a>, the Greeks self-identified as Romans (<a href="/wiki/Medieval_Greek" title="Medieval Greek">Greek</a>: <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">Ῥωμαῖοι</span> <i>Rhōmaîoi</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> By that time, the name <i>Hellenes</i> denoted pagans but was revived as an ethnonym in the 11th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Cameron_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cameron-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages">Middle Ages</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Byzantine empire">Byzantine empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Greece" title="Byzantine Greece">Byzantine Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Greeks" title="Byzantine Greeks">Byzantine Greeks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Crusade" title="Fourth Crusade">Fourth Crusade</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Frankokratia" title="Frankokratia">Frankokratia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Family_marriage.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Family_marriage.jpg/170px-Family_marriage.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="106" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/48/Family_marriage.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="240" data-file-height="149" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Family_marriage.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Scenes of marriage and family life in <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Basilios_II.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Basilios_II.jpg/170px-Basilios_II.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Basilios_II.jpg/255px-Basilios_II.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Basilios_II.jpg/340px-Basilios_II.jpg 2x" data-file-width="389" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Basilios_II.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Emperor <a href="/wiki/Basil_II" title="Basil II">Basil II</a> (11th century) is credited with reviving the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Benozzo_Gozzoli,_Pletone,_Cappella_dei_Magi.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Benozzo_Gozzoli%2C_Pletone%2C_Cappella_dei_Magi.jpg/170px-Benozzo_Gozzoli%2C_Pletone%2C_Cappella_dei_Magi.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a7/Benozzo_Gozzoli%2C_Pletone%2C_Cappella_dei_Magi.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="250" data-file-height="352" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Benozzo_Gozzoli,_Pletone,_Cappella_dei_Magi.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Gemistus_Pletho" title="Gemistus Pletho">Gemistus Pletho</a>, one of the most renowned philosophers of the late Byzantine era, a chief pioneer of the revival of Greek scholarship in Western Europe</div></div></div>
<p>There are three schools of thought regarding this Byzantine Roman identity in contemporary <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_studies" title="Byzantine studies">Byzantine scholarship</a>: The first considers "Romanity" the mode of self-identification of the subjects of a multi-ethnic empire at least up to the 12th century, where the average subject identified as Roman; a perennialist approach, which views Romanity as the medieval expression of a continuously existing Greek nation; while a third view considers the eastern Roman identity as a pre-modern national identity.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> The Byzantine Greeks' essential values were drawn from both Christianity and the Homeric tradition of ancient Greece.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Burstein_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burstein-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During most of the Middle Ages, the Byzantine Greeks self-identified as <i>Rhōmaîoi</i> (<span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">Ῥωμαῖοι</span>, "Romans", meaning <a href="/wiki/Roman_citizenship" title="Roman citizenship">citizens</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>), a term which in the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek language</a> had become synonymous with Christian Greeks.<sup id="cite_ref-Harrison268_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harrison268-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>  The Latinizing term <i>Graikoí</i> (Γραικοί, "Greeks") was also used,<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> though its use was less common, and nonexistent in official Byzantine political correspondence, prior to the Fourth Crusade of 1204.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Eastern Roman Empire</a> (today conventionally named the <i>Byzantine Empire</i>, a name not used during its own time<sup id="cite_ref-BritByz_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BritByz-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>) became increasingly influenced by Greek culture after the 7th century when Emperor <a href="/wiki/Heraclius" title="Heraclius">Heraclius</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><abbr title="reign">r</abbr>.&#160;</span> 610–641 AD) decided to make Greek the empire's official language.<sup id="cite_ref-Haldon50_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haldon50-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Although the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> recognized the Eastern Empire's claim to the Roman legacy for several centuries, after <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_III" title="Pope Leo III">Pope Leo III</a> crowned <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>, king of the <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Franks</a>, as the "<a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Roman Emperor</a>" on 25 December 800, an act which eventually led to the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>, the Latin West started to favour the Franks and began to refer to the Eastern Roman Empire largely as the <i>Empire of the Greeks</i> (<i>Imperium Graecorum</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> While this Latin term for the ancient <i><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greeks" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Greeks">Hellenes</a></i> could be used neutrally, its use by Westerners from the 9th century onwards in order to challenge Byzantine claims to <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">ancient Roman</a> heritage rendered it a derogatory <a href="/wiki/Exonym" class="mw-redirect" title="Exonym">exonym</a> for the Byzantines who barely used it, mostly in contexts relating to the West, such as texts relating to the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Florence" title="Council of Florence">Council of Florence</a>, to present the Western viewpoint.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A distinct Greek identity re-emerged in the 11th century in educated circles and became more forceful after the fall of Constantinople to the Crusaders of the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Crusade" title="Fourth Crusade">Fourth Crusade</a> in 1204.<sup id="cite_ref-BritIdent_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BritIdent-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Nicaea" title="Empire of Nicaea">Empire of Nicaea</a>, a small circle of the elite used the term "Hellene" as a term of self-identification.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> After the Byzantines recaptured Constantinople, however, in 1261, <i>Rhomaioi</i> became again dominant as a term for self-description and there are few traces of <i>Hellene</i> (Έλληνας), such as in the writings of <a href="/wiki/George_Gemistos_Plethon" class="mw-redirect" title="George Gemistos Plethon">George Gemistos Plethon</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> who abandoned Christianity and in whose writings culminated the secular tendency in the interest in the classical past.<sup id="cite_ref-BritIdent_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BritIdent-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> However, it was the combination of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Orthodox Christianity</a> with a specifically Greek identity that shaped the Greeks' notion of themselves in the empire's twilight years.<sup id="cite_ref-BritIdent_109-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BritIdent-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> In the twilight years of the Byzantine Empire, prominent Byzantine personialities proposed referring to the Byzantine Emperor as the "Emperor of the Hellenes".<sup id="cite_ref-Vasiliev_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vasiliev-112">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CareyCarey1968_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CareyCarey1968-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> These largely rhetorical expressions of Hellenic identity were confined within intellectual circles, but were continued by <a href="/wiki/Greek_scholars_in_the_Renaissance" title="Greek scholars in the Renaissance">Byzantine intellectuals who participated</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Renaissance" title="Italian Renaissance">Italian Renaissance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> The interest in the Classical Greek heritage was complemented by a renewed emphasis on <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Orthodox">Greek Orthodox</a> identity, which was reinforced in the late Medieval and Ottoman Greeks' links with their fellow Orthodox Christians in the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a>. These were further strengthened following the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Trebizond" title="Empire of Trebizond">Empire of Trebizond</a> in 1461, after which and until the second <a href="/wiki/Russo-Turkish_War_(1828%E2%80%9329)" title="Russo-Turkish War (1828–29)">Russo-Turkish War of 1828–29</a> hundreds of thousands of <a href="/wiki/Pontic_Greeks" title="Pontic Greeks">Pontic Greeks</a> fled or migrated from the <a href="/wiki/Pontic_Alps" class="mw-redirect" title="Pontic Alps">Pontic Alps</a> and <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Highlands" title="Armenian Highlands">Armenian Highlands</a> to southern Russia and the Russian <a href="/wiki/South_Caucasus" class="mw-redirect" title="South Caucasus">South Caucasus</a> (see also <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Russia" class="mw-redirect" title="Greeks in Russia">Greeks in Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Armenia" title="Greeks in Armenia">Greeks in Armenia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Georgia" title="Greeks in Georgia">Greeks in Georgia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Caucasian_Greeks" class="mw-redirect" title="Caucasian Greeks">Caucasian Greeks</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>These <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Greeks" title="Byzantine Greeks">Byzantine Greeks</a> were largely responsible for the preservation of the literature of the classical era.<sup id="cite_ref-Burstein_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burstein-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Norwich_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norwich-116">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Greek_scholars_in_the_Renaissance" title="Greek scholars in the Renaissance">Byzantine grammarians</a> were those principally responsible for carrying, in person and in writing, ancient Greek grammatical and literary studies to the West during the 15th century, giving the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Renaissance" title="Italian Renaissance">Italian Renaissance</a> a major boost.<sup id="cite_ref-BritRen_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BritRen-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotelian</a> philosophical tradition was nearly unbroken in the Greek world for almost two thousand years, until the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">Fall of Constantinople</a> in 1453.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>To the <a href="/wiki/Slavic_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Slavic people">Slavic</a> world, the Byzantine Greeks contributed by the dissemination of literacy and Christianity. The most notable example of the later was the work of the two Byzantine Greek brothers, the monks <a href="/wiki/Saints_Cyril_and_Methodius" title="Saints Cyril and Methodius">Saints Cyril and Methodius</a> from the port city of <a href="/wiki/Thessalonica" class="mw-redirect" title="Thessalonica">Thessalonica</a>, capital of the <a href="/wiki/Theme_of_Thessalonica" class="mw-redirect" title="Theme of Thessalonica">theme of Thessalonica</a>, who are credited today with formalizing the <a href="/wiki/Glagolitic_alphabet" class="mw-redirect" title="Glagolitic alphabet">first Slavic alphabet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ottoman_Empire">Ottoman Empire</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Greeks" title="Ottoman Greeks">Ottoman Greeks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phanariotes" class="mw-redirect" title="Phanariotes">Phanariotes</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Basilius_Bessarion_-_Imagines_philologorum.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Basilius_Bessarion_-_Imagines_philologorum.jpg/150px-Basilius_Bessarion_-_Imagines_philologorum.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Basilius_Bessarion_-_Imagines_philologorum.jpg/225px-Basilius_Bessarion_-_Imagines_philologorum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Basilius_Bessarion_-_Imagines_philologorum.jpg/299px-Basilius_Bessarion_-_Imagines_philologorum.jpg 2x" data-file-width="441" data-file-height="539" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Basilius_Bessarion_-_Imagines_philologorum.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Byzantine scholar and cardinal <a href="/wiki/Basilios_Bessarion" title="Basilios Bessarion">Basilios Bessarion</a> (1395/1403–1472) played a key role in transmitting classical knowledge to the Western Europe, contributing to the Renaissance</div></div></div>
<p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">Fall of Constantinople</a> on 29 May 1453, many Greeks sought better employment and education opportunities by leaving for the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">West</a>, particularly <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BritRen_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BritRen-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Greeks are greatly credited for the European cultural revolution, later called, the Renaissance. In Greek-inhabited territory itself, Greeks came to play a leading role in the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, due in part to the fact that the central hub of the empire, politically, culturally, and socially, was based on <a href="/wiki/Western_Thrace" title="Western Thrace">Western Thrace</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_Macedonia" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Macedonia">Greek Macedonia</a>, both in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Greece" title="Northern Greece">Northern Greece</a>, and of course was centred on the mainly Greek-populated, former Byzantine capital, <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>. As a direct consequence of this situation, Greek-speakers came to play a hugely important role in the Ottoman trading and diplomatic establishment, as well as in the church. Added to this, in the first half of the Ottoman period men of Greek origin made up a significant proportion of the Ottoman army, navy, and state bureaucracy, having been levied as adolescents (along with especially <a href="/wiki/Albanians" title="Albanians">Albanians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Serbs" title="Serbs">Serbs</a>) into Ottoman service through the <a href="/wiki/Devshirme" title="Devshirme">devshirme</a>. Many Ottomans of Greek (or Albanian or Serb) origin were therefore to be found within the Ottoman forces which governed the provinces, from Ottoman Egypt, to Ottomans occupied <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>, frequently as provincial governors.
</p><p>For those that remained under the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Millet_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="Millet (Ottoman Empire)">millet system</a>, religion was the defining characteristic of national groups (<i>milletler</i>), so the <a href="/wiki/Exonym" class="mw-redirect" title="Exonym">exonym</a> "Greeks" (<i>Rumlar</i> from the name Rhomaioi) was applied by the Ottomans to all members of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Orthodox Church</a>, regardless of their language or ethnic origin.<sup id="cite_ref-Mazower_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mazower-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> speakers were the only ethnic group to actually call themselves <i>Romioi</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> (as opposed to being so named by others) and, at least those educated, considered their ethnicity (<i>genos</i>) to be Hellenic.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> There were, however, many Greeks who escaped the second-class status of Christians inherent in the Ottoman <a href="/wiki/Millet" title="Millet">millet</a> system, according to which Muslims were explicitly awarded senior status and preferential treatment. These Greeks either emigrated, particularly to their fellow Orthodox Christian protector, the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a>, or simply converted to Islam, often only very superficially and whilst remaining <a href="/wiki/Crypto-Christian" class="mw-redirect" title="Crypto-Christian">crypto-Christian</a>. The most notable examples of large-scale conversion to Turkish Islam among those today defined as <a href="/wiki/Greek_Muslims" title="Greek Muslims">Greek Muslims</a>—excluding those who had to convert as a matter of course on being recruited through the <a href="/wiki/Devshirme" title="Devshirme">devshirme</a>—were to be found in <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a> (<a href="/wiki/Cretan_Turks" title="Cretan Turks">Cretan Turks</a>), <a href="/wiki/Greek_Macedonia" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Macedonia">Greek Macedonia</a> (for example among the <a href="/wiki/Vallahades" title="Vallahades">Vallahades</a> of western <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(Greece)" title="Macedonia (Greece)">Macedonia</a>), and among <a href="/wiki/Pontic_Greeks" title="Pontic Greeks">Pontic Greeks</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Pontic_Alps" class="mw-redirect" title="Pontic Alps">Pontic Alps</a> and <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Highlands" title="Armenian Highlands">Armenian Highlands</a>. Several Ottoman sultans and princes were also of part Greek origin, with mothers who were either Greek concubines or princesses from Byzantine noble families, one famous example being sultan <a href="/wiki/Selim_I" title="Selim I">Selim the Grim</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><abbr title="reign">r</abbr>.&#160;</span> 1517–1520), whose mother <a href="/wiki/G%C3%BClbahar_Hatun" class="mw-redirect" title="Gülbahar Hatun">Gülbahar Hatun</a> was a <a href="/wiki/Pontic_Greek" title="Pontic Greek">Pontic Greek</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Adamantios_Korais.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Adamantios_Korais.jpg/140px-Adamantios_Korais.jpg" decoding="async" width="140" height="190" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Adamantios_Korais.jpg/210px-Adamantios_Korais.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Adamantios_Korais.jpg/280px-Adamantios_Korais.jpg 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="407" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Adamantios_Korais.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Adamantios_Korais" title="Adamantios Korais">Adamantios Korais</a>, leading figure of the <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek_Enlightenment" title="Modern Greek Enlightenment">Modern Greek Enlightenment</a></div></div></div>
<p>The roots of Greek success in the Ottoman Empire can be traced to the Greek tradition of education and commerce exemplified in the <a href="/wiki/Phanariotes" class="mw-redirect" title="Phanariotes">Phanariotes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BritB_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BritB-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> It was the wealth of the extensive merchant class that provided the material basis for the intellectual revival that was the prominent feature of Greek life in the half century and more leading to the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_War_of_Independence" title="Greek War of Independence">Greek War of Independence</a> in 1821.<sup id="cite_ref-BritMerchant_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BritMerchant-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Not coincidentally, on the eve of 1821, the three most important centres of Greek learning were situated in <a href="/wiki/Chios" title="Chios">Chios</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smyrna" title="Smyrna">Smyrna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ayvalik" class="mw-redirect" title="Ayvalik">Aivali</a>, all three major centres of Greek commerce.<sup id="cite_ref-BritMerchant_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BritMerchant-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Greek success was also favoured by Greek domination in the leadership of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Orthodox">Eastern Orthodox</a> church.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern">Modern</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek_Enlightenment" title="Modern Greek Enlightenment">Modern Greek Enlightenment</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_War_of_Independence" title="Greek War of Independence">Greek War of Independence</a></div>
<p>The movement of the Greek enlightenment, the Greek expression of the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a>, contributed not only in the promotion of education, culture and printing among the Greeks, but also in the case of independence from the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottoman empire">Ottomans</a>, and the restoration of the term "Hellene". <a href="/wiki/Adamantios_Korais" title="Adamantios Korais">Adamantios Korais</a>, probably the most important intellectual of the movement, advocated the use of the term "Hellene" (Έλληνας) or "Graikos" (Γραικός) in the place of <i>Romiós</i>, that was sawn negatively by him.
</p><p>The relationship between ethnic Greek identity and <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church" title="Greek Orthodox Church">Greek Orthodox</a> religion continued after the creation of the modern Greek nation-state in 1830. According to the second article of the first <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Greece" title="Constitution of Greece">Greek constitution</a> of 1822, a Greek was defined as any native Christian resident of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Greece_(Wittelsbach)" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingdom of Greece (Wittelsbach)">Kingdom of Greece</a>, a clause removed by 1840.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> A century later, when the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Lausanne" title="Treaty of Lausanne">Treaty of Lausanne</a> was signed between <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> in 1923, the two countries agreed to use religion as the determinant for ethnic identity for the purposes of population exchange, although most of the Greeks displaced (over a million of the total 1.5 million) had already been driven out by the time the agreement was signed.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;note 2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Greek_genocide" title="Greek genocide">Greek genocide</a>, in particular the harsh removal of Pontian Greeks from the southern shore area of the Black Sea, contemporaneous with and following the failed Greek <a href="/wiki/Greco-Turkish_War_(1919%E2%80%931922)" title="Greco-Turkish War (1919–1922)">Asia Minor Campaign</a>, was part of this process of <a href="/wiki/Turkification" title="Turkification">Turkification</a> of the Ottoman Empire and the placement of its economy and trade, then largely in Greek hands under ethnic Turkish control.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Identity">Identity</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hermes_the_scholar.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Hermes_the_scholar.jpg/140px-Hermes_the_scholar.jpg" decoding="async" width="140" height="221" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fd/Hermes_the_scholar.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="190" data-file-height="300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hermes_the_scholar.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The cover of <i><a href="/wiki/Hermes_o_Logios" title="Hermes o Logios">Hermes o Logios</a></i>, a Greek literary publication of the late 18th and early 19th century in <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a> with major contribution to the <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek_Enlightenment" title="Modern Greek Enlightenment">Modern Greek Enlightenment</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The terms used to define Greekness have varied throughout history but were never limited or completely identified with membership to a Greek state.<sup id="cite_ref-Broome_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Broome-131">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> gave a famous account of what defined Greek (Hellenic) ethnic identity in his day, enumerating 
</p>
<ol><li>shared <a href="/wiki/Kinship_and_descent" class="mw-redirect" title="Kinship and descent">descent</a> (ὅμαιμον - <i>homaimon</i>, "of the same blood"),<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>shared <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a> (ὁμόγλωσσον - <i>homoglōsson</i>, "speaking the same language")<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>shared <a href="/wiki/Sanctuaries" class="mw-redirect" title="Sanctuaries">sanctuaries</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sacrifices" class="mw-redirect" title="Sacrifices">sacrifices</a> (Greek: θεῶν ἱδρύματά τε κοινὰ καὶ θυσίαι - <i>theōn hidrumata te koina kai thusiai</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>shared <a href="/wiki/Mores" title="Mores">customs</a> (Greek: ἤθεα ὁμότροπα - <i>ēthea homotropa</i>, "customs of like fashion").<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>By Western standards, the term <i>Greeks</i> has traditionally referred to any native speakers of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek language</a>, whether <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greek_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Mycenaean Greek language">Mycenaean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Greek" title="Medieval Greek">Byzantine</a> or <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek" title="Modern Greek">modern Greek</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mazower_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mazower-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Greeks" title="Byzantine Greeks">Byzantine Greeks</a> self-identified as <i>Romaioi</i> ("Romans"), <i>Graikoi</i> ("Greeks") and <i>Christianoi</i> ("Christians") since they were the political heirs of <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">imperial Rome</a>, the descendants of their <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greeks" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Greeks">classical Greek forebears</a> and followers of the <a href="/wiki/Apostles" title="Apostles">Apostles</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> during the mid-to-late Byzantine period (11th–13th century), a growing number of Byzantine Greek intellectuals deemed themselves <i>Hellenes</i> although for most Greek-speakers, "Hellene" still meant pagan.<sup id="cite_ref-Cameron_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cameron-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> On the eve of the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">Fall of Constantinople</a> the <a href="/wiki/Constantine_XI" class="mw-redirect" title="Constantine XI">Last Emperor</a> urged his soldiers to remember that they were the descendants of Greeks and Romans.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Before the establishment of the modern Greek nation-state, the link between ancient and modern Greeks was emphasized by the scholars of Greek Enlightenment especially by Rigas Feraios. In his "Political Constitution", he addresses to the nation as "the people descendant of the Greeks".<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/History_of_Modern_Greece" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Modern Greece">modern Greek state</a> was created in 1829, when the Greeks liberated a part of their historic homelands, <a href="/wiki/Peloponnese" title="Peloponnese">Peloponnese</a>, from the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> The large <a href="/wiki/Greek_diaspora" title="Greek diaspora">Greek diaspora</a> and merchant class were instrumental in transmitting the ideas of western <a href="/wiki/Romantic_nationalism" title="Romantic nationalism">romantic nationalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philhellenism" title="Philhellenism">philhellenism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-BritMerchant_126-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BritMerchant-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> which together with the conception of Hellenism, formulated during the last centuries of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>, formed the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Diafotismos" class="mw-redirect" title="Diafotismos">Diafotismos</a> and the current conception of Hellenism.<sup id="cite_ref-BritIdent_109-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BritIdent-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mazower_122-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mazower-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Greeks today are a nation in the meaning of an <i><a href="/wiki/Ethnic_group" title="Ethnic group">ethnos</a></i>, defined by possessing <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Greece" title="Culture of Greece">Greek culture</a> and having a Greek <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">mother tongue</a>, not by citizenship, race, and religion or by being subjects of any particular state.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> In ancient and medieval times and to a lesser extent today the Greek term was <i><a href="/wiki/Genos" title="Genos">genos</a></i>, which also indicates a common ancestry.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Names">Names</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Achaeans_(Homer)" title="Achaeans (Homer)">Achaeans (Homer)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Names_of_the_Greeks" title="Names of the Greeks">Names of the Greeks</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ancient_Regions_Mainland_Greece.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Ancient_Regions_Mainland_Greece.png/220px-Ancient_Regions_Mainland_Greece.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Ancient_Regions_Mainland_Greece.png/330px-Ancient_Regions_Mainland_Greece.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Ancient_Regions_Mainland_Greece.png/440px-Ancient_Regions_Mainland_Greece.png 2x" data-file-width="950" data-file-height="1100" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ancient_Regions_Mainland_Greece.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map showing the major regions of mainland ancient Greece, and adjacent "barbarian" lands.</div></div></div>
<p>Greeks and Greek-speakers have used different names to refer to themselves collectively. The term <em>Achaeans</em> (Ἀχαιοί) is one of the <a href="/wiki/Names_of_the_Greeks" title="Names of the Greeks">collective names</a> for the Greeks in <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Odyssey" title="Odyssey">Odyssey</a></i> (the Homeric "long-haired Achaeans" would have been a part of the <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_civilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Mycenaean civilization">Mycenaean civilization</a> that dominated Greece from <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1600 BC until 1100 BC). The other common names are <em>Danaans</em> (Δαναοί) and <em>Argives</em> (Ἀργεῖοι) while <em>Panhellenes</em> (Πανέλληνες) and <em>Hellenes</em> (Ἕλληνες) both <a href="/wiki/Hapax_legomenon" title="Hapax legomenon">appear only once</a> in the <i>Iliad</i>;<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> all of these terms were used, synonymously, to denote a common Greek identity.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> In the historical period, Herodotus identified the <a href="/wiki/Achaea_(ancient_region)" title="Achaea (ancient region)">Achaeans</a> of the northern <a href="/wiki/Peloponnese" title="Peloponnese">Peloponnese</a> as descendants of the earlier, Homeric Achaeans.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a> refers to the "Hellenes" (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;h&#39; in &#39;hi&#39;">h</span><span title="/ɛ/: &#39;e&#39; in &#39;dress&#39;">ɛ</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/iː/: &#39;ee&#39; in &#39;fleece&#39;">iː</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="&#39;z&#39; in &#39;zoom&#39;">z</span></span>/</a></span></span>) as a relatively small tribe settled in Thessalic <a href="/wiki/Phthia" title="Phthia">Phthia</a>, with its warriors under the command of <a href="/wiki/Achilleus" class="mw-redirect" title="Achilleus">Achilleus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Parian_Chronicle" title="Parian Chronicle">Parian Chronicle</a> says that Phthia was the homeland of the Hellenes and that this name was given to those previously called Greeks (<span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">Γραικοί</span>).<sup id="cite_ref-Parian-Chronicle_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parian-Chronicle-153">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hellen" title="Hellen">Hellen</a>, the patriarch of the Hellenes who ruled around Phthia, was the son of <a href="/wiki/Pyrrha" class="mw-redirect" title="Pyrrha">Pyrrha</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deucalion" title="Deucalion">Deucalion</a>, the only survivors after the <a href="/wiki/Flood_myth" title="Flood myth">Great Deluge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> The Greek philosopher <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> names ancient <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Hellas</a> as an area in <a href="/wiki/Epirus" title="Epirus">Epirus</a> between <a href="/wiki/Dodona" title="Dodona">Dodona</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Achelous" title="Achelous">Achelous</a> river, the location of the Great Deluge of <a href="/wiki/Deucalion" title="Deucalion">Deucalion</a>, a land occupied by the <a href="/wiki/Selloi" title="Selloi">Selloi</a> and the "Greeks" who later came to be known as "Hellenes".<sup id="cite_ref-Aristotle_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aristotle-155">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> In the Homeric tradition, the Selloi were the priests of Dodonian Zeus.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Hesiod" title="Hesiod">Hesiodic</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Catalogue_of_Women" title="Catalogue of Women">Catalogue of Women</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Graecus" title="Graecus">Graecus</a> is presented as the son of Zeus and <a href="/wiki/Pandora_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Pandora II">Pandora II</a>, sister of <a href="/wiki/Hellen" title="Hellen">Hellen</a> the patriarch of the Hellenes.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Parian_Chronicle" title="Parian Chronicle">Parian Chronicle</a>, when <a href="/wiki/Deucalion" title="Deucalion">Deucalion</a> became king of Phthia, the <em>Graikoi</em> (Γραικοί) were named Hellenes.<sup id="cite_ref-Parian-Chronicle_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parian-Chronicle-153">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> notes in his <i>Meteorologica</i> that the Hellenes were related to the Graikoi.<sup id="cite_ref-Aristotle_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aristotle-155">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Continuity">Continuity</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Bracca_(cropped).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Bracca_%28cropped%29.JPG/180px-Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Bracca_%28cropped%29.JPG" decoding="async" width="180" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Bracca_%28cropped%29.JPG/270px-Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Bracca_%28cropped%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Bracca_%28cropped%29.JPG/360px-Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Bracca_%28cropped%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1380" data-file-height="1323" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Bracca_(cropped).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alexander the Great in <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Emperor" class="mw-redirect" title="Byzantine Emperor">Byzantine Emperor</a>'s clothes, by a manuscript depicting scenes from his life (between 1204-1453)</div></div></div>
