<p>The most obvious link between modern and ancient Greeks is their language, which has a documented tradition from at least the 14th century BC to the present day, albeit with a break during the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Dark_Ages" title="Greek Dark Ages">Greek Dark Ages</a> (11th- 8th cent. BC, though the <a href="/wiki/Cypriot_syllabary" title="Cypriot syllabary">Cypriot syllabary</a> was in use during this period).<sup id="cite_ref-Adrados_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adrados-158">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Scholars compare its continuity of tradition to <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a> alone.<sup id="cite_ref-Adrados_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adrados-158">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Browning_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-159">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Since its inception, Hellenism was primarily a matter of common culture and the national continuity of the Greek world is a lot more certain than its demographic.<sup id="cite_ref-Roberts1_38-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts1-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ADS_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ADS-160">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Yet, Hellenism also embodied an ancestral dimension through aspects of Athenian literature that developed and influenced ideas of descent based on autochthony.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> During the later years of the Eastern Roman Empire, areas such as <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">Ionia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> experienced a Hellenic revival in language, philosophy, and literature and on classical models of thought and scholarship.<sup id="cite_ref-ADS_160-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ADS-160">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> This revival provided a powerful impetus to the sense of cultural affinity with ancient Greece and its classical heritage.<sup id="cite_ref-ADS_160-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ADS-160">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Throughout their history, the Greeks have retained their language and <a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">alphabet</a>, certain values and cultural traditions, customs, a sense of religious and cultural difference and exclusion (the word <i><a href="/wiki/Barbarian" title="Barbarian">barbarian</a></i> was used by 12th-century historian <a href="/wiki/Anna_Komnene" title="Anna Komnene">Anna Komnene</a> to describe non-Greek speakers),<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> a sense of Greek identity and common sense of ethnicity despite the undeniable socio-political changes of the past two millennia.<sup id="cite_ref-ADS_160-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ADS-160">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> In recent anthropological studies, both ancient and modern Greek osteological samples were analyzed demonstrating a bio-genetic affinity and continuity shared between both groups.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Greece" title="Demographics of Greece">Demographics of Greece</a> and <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Cyprus" title="Demographics of Cyprus">Demographics of Cyprus</a></div>
<p>Today, Greeks are the majority ethnic group in the <a href="/wiki/Hellenic_Republic" class="mw-redirect" title="Hellenic Republic">Hellenic Republic</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Greece_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greece-165">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> where they constitute 93% of the country's population,<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Cyprus" class="mw-redirect" title="Republic of Cyprus">Republic of Cyprus</a> where they make up 78% of the island's population (excluding Turkish settlers in the occupied part of the country).<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-167">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> Greek populations have not traditionally exhibited high rates of growth; a large percentage of Greek population growth since Greece's foundation in 1832 was attributed to annexation of new territories, as well as the influx of 1.5 million Greek refugees after the <a href="/wiki/Population_exchange_between_Greece_and_Turkey" title="Population exchange between Greece and Turkey">1923 population exchange</a> between Greece and Turkey.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> About 80% of the population of Greece is urban, with 28% concentrated in the city of Athens.<sup id="cite_ref-EconWorld_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EconWorld-169">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Greeks from Cyprus have a similar history of emigration, usually to the English-speaking world because of the island's colonization by the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>. Waves of <a href="/wiki/Emigration" title="Emigration">emigration</a> followed the <a href="/wiki/Turkish_invasion_of_Cyprus" title="Turkish invasion of Cyprus">Turkish invasion of Cyprus</a> in 1974, while the population decreased between mid-1974 and 1977 as a result of emigration, war losses, and a temporary decline in fertility.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica-Cyprus_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-Cyprus-170">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_cleansing" title="Ethnic cleansing">ethnic cleansing</a> of a third of the Greek population of the island in 1974,<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> there was also an increase in the number of Greek Cypriots leaving, especially for the Middle East, which contributed to a decrease in population that tapered off in the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica-Cyprus_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-Cyprus-170">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> Today more than two-thirds of the Greek population in Cyprus is urban.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica-Cyprus_170-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-Cyprus-170">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There is a sizeable Greek minority of approximately 200,000 people in <a href="/wiki/Greek_minority_in_Albania" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek minority in Albania">Albania</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jeffries_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffries-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The Greek minority of <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Turkey" title="Greeks in Turkey">Turkey</a>, which numbered upwards of 200,000 people after the 1923 exchange, has now dwindled to a few thousand, after the 1955 <a href="/wiki/Istanbul_Pogrom" class="mw-redirect" title="Istanbul Pogrom">Constantinople Pogrom</a> and other state sponsored violence and discrimination.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> This effectively ended, though not entirely, the three-thousand-year-old presence of Hellenism in Asia Minor.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> There are smaller Greek minorities in the rest of the Balkan countries, the <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Lebanon" title="Greeks in Lebanon">Levant</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Georgia" title="Greeks in Georgia">Black Sea</a> states, remnants of the Old <a href="/wiki/Greek_Diaspora" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Diaspora">Greek Diaspora</a> (pre-19th century).<sup id="cite_ref-Prevelakis_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prevelakis-176">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diaspora">Diaspora</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Greek_diaspora" title="Greek diaspora">Greek diaspora</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:50_largest_Greek_diaspora.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/50_largest_Greek_diaspora.png/280px-50_largest_Greek_diaspora.png" decoding="async" width="280" height="123" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/50_largest_Greek_diaspora.png/420px-50_largest_Greek_diaspora.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/50_largest_Greek_diaspora.png/560px-50_largest_Greek_diaspora.png 2x" data-file-width="1425" data-file-height="625" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:50_largest_Greek_diaspora.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Greek diaspora (20th century).</div></div></div>
<p>The total number of Greeks living outside Greece and Cyprus today is a contentious issue. Where Census figures are available, they show around 3 million Greeks outside <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>. Estimates provided by the <a href="/wiki/SAE_-_World_Council_of_Hellenes_Abroad" class="mw-redirect" title="SAE - World Council of Hellenes Abroad">SAE - World Council of Hellenes Abroad</a> put the figure at around 7 million worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> According to George Prevelakis of <a href="/wiki/Sorbonne_University" title="Sorbonne University">Sorbonne University</a>, the number is closer to just below 5 million.<sup id="cite_ref-Prevelakis_176-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prevelakis-176">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> Integration, intermarriage, and loss of the Greek language influence the self-identification of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_diaspora" title="Greek diaspora">Omogeneia</a>. Important centres of the New Greek Diaspora today are <a href="/wiki/British_Greeks" class="mw-redirect" title="British Greeks">London</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_Americans" title="Greek Americans">New York</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_Australians" title="Greek Australians">Melbourne</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_Canadians" title="Greek Canadians">Toronto</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Prevelakis_176-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prevelakis-176">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> In 2010, the Hellenic Parliament introduced a law that enables Diaspora Greeks in Greece to vote in the elections of the Greek state.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> This law was later repealed in early 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient">Ancient</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Colonies_in_antiquity" title="Colonies in antiquity">Colonies in antiquity</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Griechischen_und_ph%C3%B6nizischen_Kolonien.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Griechischen_und_ph%C3%B6nizischen_Kolonien.jpg/280px-Griechischen_und_ph%C3%B6nizischen_Kolonien.jpg" decoding="async" width="280" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Griechischen_und_ph%C3%B6nizischen_Kolonien.jpg/420px-Griechischen_und_ph%C3%B6nizischen_Kolonien.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Griechischen_und_ph%C3%B6nizischen_Kolonien.jpg/560px-Griechischen_und_ph%C3%B6nizischen_Kolonien.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2836" data-file-height="1616" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Griechischen_und_ph%C3%B6nizischen_Kolonien.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Greek colonization in antiquity.</div></div></div>
<p>In ancient times, the trading and colonizing activities of the Greek tribes and city states spread the Greek culture, religion and language around the Mediterranean and Black Sea basins, especially in <a href="/wiki/Magna_Graecia" title="Magna Graecia">Sicily and southern Italy</a> (also known as <a href="/wiki/Magna_Grecia" class="mw-redirect" title="Magna Grecia">Magna Grecia</a>), Spain, the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Marseille" title="History of Marseille">south of France</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pontian_Greeks" class="mw-redirect" title="Pontian Greeks">Black sea coasts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Apoikiai_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apoikiai-180">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> Under Alexander the Great's empire and successor states, Greek and Hellenizing ruling classes were established in the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Seleucid Kingdom">Middle East</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indo-Greek_Kingdom" title="Indo-Greek Kingdom">India</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_dynasty" title="Ptolemaic dynasty">Egypt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Apoikiai_180-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apoikiai-180">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic period</a> is characterized by a new wave of Greek colonization that established Greek cities and kingdoms in <a href="/wiki/Dayuan" title="Dayuan">Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyrene,_Libya" title="Cyrene, Libya">Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Under the Roman Empire, easier movement of people spread Greeks across the Empire and in the eastern territories, Greek became the <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a> rather than <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Haldon50_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haldon50-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The modern-day <a href="/wiki/Griko_people" title="Griko people">Griko community</a> of southern Italy, numbering about 60,000,<sup id="cite_ref-Grecia-Salentina_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grecia-Salentina-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bellinello_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bellinello-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> may represent a living remnant of the ancient Greek populations of Italy.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_2">Modern</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Distribution_Of_Races_1918_National_Geographic.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Distribution_Of_Races_1918_National_Geographic.jpg/280px-Distribution_Of_Races_1918_National_Geographic.jpg" decoding="async" width="280" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Distribution_Of_Races_1918_National_Geographic.jpg/420px-Distribution_Of_Races_1918_National_Geographic.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Distribution_Of_Races_1918_National_Geographic.jpg/560px-Distribution_Of_Races_1918_National_Geographic.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2267" data-file-height="1214" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Distribution_Of_Races_1918_National_Geographic.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Distribution of ethnic groups in 1918, National Geographic</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Constantine_Cavafy_with_cane_and_hat_in_hand_Photograph_dated_1896_Alexandria_Egypt.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Constantine_Cavafy_with_cane_and_hat_in_hand_Photograph_dated_1896_Alexandria_Egypt.jpg/160px-Constantine_Cavafy_with_cane_and_hat_in_hand_Photograph_dated_1896_Alexandria_Egypt.jpg" decoding="async" width="160" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Constantine_Cavafy_with_cane_and_hat_in_hand_Photograph_dated_1896_Alexandria_Egypt.jpg/240px-Constantine_Cavafy_with_cane_and_hat_in_hand_Photograph_dated_1896_Alexandria_Egypt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Constantine_Cavafy_with_cane_and_hat_in_hand_Photograph_dated_1896_Alexandria_Egypt.jpg/320px-Constantine_Cavafy_with_cane_and_hat_in_hand_Photograph_dated_1896_Alexandria_Egypt.jpg 2x" data-file-width="914" data-file-height="1364" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Constantine_Cavafy_with_cane_and_hat_in_hand_Photograph_dated_1896_Alexandria_Egypt.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Poet <a href="/wiki/Constantine_P._Cavafy" title="Constantine P. Cavafy">Constantine P. Cavafy</a>, a native of <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a></div></div></div>
<p>During and after the <a href="/wiki/Greek_War_of_Independence" title="Greek War of Independence">Greek War of Independence</a>, Greeks of the diaspora were important in establishing the fledgling state, raising funds and awareness abroad.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Greek merchant families already had contacts in other countries and during the disturbances many set up home around the Mediterranean (notably Marseilles in <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_France" title="Greeks in France">France</a>, Livorno in <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Italy" title="Greeks in Italy">Italy</a>, Alexandria in <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Egypt" title="Greeks in Egypt">Egypt</a>), <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Russia" class="mw-redirect" title="Greeks in Russia">Russia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Odessa" title="Odessa">Odessa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a>), and <a href="/wiki/British_Greeks" class="mw-redirect" title="British Greeks">Britain</a> (London and Liverpool) from where they traded, typically in textiles and grain.<sup id="cite_ref-Diaspora_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Diaspora-183">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> Businesses frequently comprised the extended family, and with them they brought schools teaching Greek and the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church" title="Greek Orthodox Church">Greek Orthodox Church</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Diaspora_183-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Diaspora-183">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As markets changed and they became more established, some families grew their operations to become <a href="/wiki/Greek_shipping" title="Greek shipping">shippers</a>, financed through the local Greek community, notably with the aid of the <a href="/wiki/Ralli_Brothers" title="Ralli Brothers">Ralli</a> or <a href="/wiki/Panayis_Athanase_Vagliano" title="Panayis Athanase Vagliano">Vagliano Brothers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kardasis_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kardasis-184">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> With economic success, the Diaspora expanded further across the <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Syria" title="Greeks in Syria">Levant</a>, North Africa, India and the USA.<sup id="cite_ref-Kardasis_184-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kardasis-184">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clogg_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clogg-185">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 20th century, many Greeks left their traditional homelands for economic reasons resulting in large migrations from Greece and Cyprus to the <a href="/wiki/Greek_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek American">United States</a>, Great Britain, Australia, Canada, <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Germany" title="Greeks in Germany">Germany</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_South_Africa" title="Greeks in South Africa">South Africa</a>, especially after the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a> (1939–1945), the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Civil_War" title="Greek Civil War">Greek Civil War</a> (1946–1949), and the <a href="/wiki/Turkish_Invasion_of_Cyprus" class="mw-redirect" title="Turkish Invasion of Cyprus">Turkish Invasion of Cyprus</a> in 1974.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While official figures remain scarce, polls and anecdotal evidence point to renewed Greek emigration as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_financial_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek financial crisis">Greek financial crisis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> According to data published by the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Statistical_Office_of_Germany" title="Federal Statistical Office of Germany">Federal Statistical Office of Germany</a> in 2011, 23,800 Greeks emigrated to Germany, a significant increase over the previous year. By comparison, about 9,000 Greeks emigrated to Germany in 2009 and 12,000 in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Greece" title="Culture of Greece">Culture of Greece</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Greece" title="Culture of Greece">Greek culture</a> has evolved over thousands of years, with its beginning in the Mycenaean civilization, continuing through the classical era, the Hellenistic period, the Roman and Byzantine periods and was profoundly affected by Christianity, which it in turn influenced and shaped.<sup id="cite_ref-Christianity-Hellenism_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christianity-Hellenism-190">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Greeks" title="Ottoman Greeks">Ottoman Greeks</a> had to endure through several centuries of adversity that culminated in <a href="/wiki/Greek_genocide" title="Greek genocide">genocide</a> in the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-IAGS_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IAGS-191">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Diafotismos" class="mw-redirect" title="Diafotismos">Diafotismos</a> is credited with revitalizing Greek culture and giving birth to the synthesis of ancient and medieval elements that characterize it today.<sup id="cite_ref-BritIdent_109-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BritIdent-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mazower_122-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mazower-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek language</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_language_question" title="Greek language question">Greek language question</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NAMA_Alphabet_grec.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/NAMA_Alphabet_grec.jpg/220px-NAMA_Alphabet_grec.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="192" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/NAMA_Alphabet_grec.jpg/330px-NAMA_Alphabet_grec.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/NAMA_Alphabet_grec.jpg/440px-NAMA_Alphabet_grec.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1398" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NAMA_Alphabet_grec.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early Greek alphabet, c. 8th century BC</div></div></div>
<p>Most Greeks speak the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek language</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Hellenic_languages" title="Hellenic languages">independent branch</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European languages</a>, with its closest relations possibly being <a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">Armenian</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Graeco-Armenian" title="Graeco-Armenian">Graeco-Armenian</a>) or the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranian_languages" title="Indo-Iranian languages">Indo-Iranian languages</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Graeco-Aryan" title="Graeco-Aryan">Graeco-Aryan</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Adrados_158-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adrados-158">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> It has the longest documented history of any living language and <a href="/wiki/Greek_literature" title="Greek literature">Greek literature</a> has a continuous history of over 2,500 years.<sup id="cite_ref-BritLit_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BritLit-193">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> The oldest inscriptions in Greek are in the <a href="/wiki/Linear_B" title="Linear B">Linear B</a> script, dated as far back as 1450 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> Following the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Dark_Ages" title="Greek Dark Ages">Greek Dark Ages</a>, from which written records are absent, the <a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">Greek alphabet</a> appears in the 9th-8th century BC. The Greek alphabet derived from the <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_alphabet" title="Phoenician alphabet">Phoenician alphabet</a>, and in turn became the parent alphabet of the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic" class="mw-redirect" title="Cyrillic">Cyrillic</a>, and several other alphabets. The earliest Greek literary works are the <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homeric epics</a>, variously dated from the 8th to the 6th century BC. Notable scientific and mathematical works include  <a href="/wiki/Euclid%27s_Elements" title="Euclid&#39;s Elements">Euclid's Elements</a>, Ptolemy's <a href="/wiki/Almagest" title="Almagest">Almagest</a>, and others. The <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> was originally written in <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Koine Greek</a>.
</p><p>Greek demonstrates several linguistic features that are shared with other <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_the_Balkans" title="Languages of the Balkans">Balkan languages</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">Albanian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_language" title="Bulgarian language">Bulgarian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Romance_languages" title="Eastern Romance languages">Eastern Romance languages</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Balkan_sprachbund" title="Balkan sprachbund">Balkan sprachbund</a>), and has absorbed many foreign words, primarily of Western European and <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a> origin.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> Because of the movements of <a href="/wiki/Philhellenism" title="Philhellenism">Philhellenism</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Diafotismos" class="mw-redirect" title="Diafotismos">Diafotismos</a> in the 19th century, which emphasized the modern Greeks' ancient heritage, these foreign influences were excluded from official use via the creation of <a href="/wiki/Katharevousa" title="Katharevousa">Katharevousa</a>, a somewhat artificial form of Greek purged of all foreign influence and words, as the official language of the Greek state. In 1976, however, the <a href="/wiki/Hellenic_Parliament" title="Hellenic Parliament">Hellenic Parliament</a> voted to make the spoken <a href="/wiki/Dimotiki" class="mw-redirect" title="Dimotiki">Dimotiki</a> the official language, making Katharevousa obsolete.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek" title="Modern Greek">Modern Greek</a> has, in addition to Standard Modern Greek or Dimotiki, a wide <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Modern_Greek" title="Varieties of Modern Greek">variety of dialects</a> of varying levels of mutual intelligibility, including <a href="/wiki/Cypriot_Greek" title="Cypriot Greek">Cypriot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pontic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Pontic language">Pontic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cappadocian_Greek" title="Cappadocian Greek">Cappadocian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Griko_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Griko language">Griko</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tsakonian_language" title="Tsakonian language">Tsakonian</a> (the only surviving representative of ancient <a href="/wiki/Doric_Greek" title="Doric Greek">Doric Greek</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Yevanic_language" title="Yevanic language">Yevanic</a> is the language of the <a href="/wiki/Romaniotes" class="mw-redirect" title="Romaniotes">Romaniotes</a>, and survives in small communities in Greece, New York and Israel. In addition to Greek, many Greeks in Greece and the Diaspora are bilingual in other languages or dialects such as English, <a href="/wiki/Arvanitika" title="Arvanitika">Arvanitika</a>/<a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">Albanian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aromanian_language" title="Aromanian language">Aromanian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slavic_dialects_of_Greece" title="Slavic dialects of Greece">Macedonian Slavic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a> and Turkish.<sup id="cite_ref-Adrados_158-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adrados-158">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_ancient_Greece" class="mw-redirect" title="Religion in ancient Greece">Religion in ancient Greece</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church" title="Greek Orthodox Church">Greek Orthodox Church</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Christ_Pantocrator_mosaic_from_Hagia_Sophia_2744_x_2900_pixels_3.1_MB.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Christ_Pantocrator_mosaic_from_Hagia_Sophia_2744_x_2900_pixels_3.1_MB.jpg/200px-Christ_Pantocrator_mosaic_from_Hagia_Sophia_2744_x_2900_pixels_3.1_MB.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="211" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Christ_Pantocrator_mosaic_from_Hagia_Sophia_2744_x_2900_pixels_3.1_MB.jpg/300px-Christ_Pantocrator_mosaic_from_Hagia_Sophia_2744_x_2900_pixels_3.1_MB.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Christ_Pantocrator_mosaic_from_Hagia_Sophia_2744_x_2900_pixels_3.1_MB.jpg/400px-Christ_Pantocrator_mosaic_from_Hagia_Sophia_2744_x_2900_pixels_3.1_MB.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2744" data-file-height="2900" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Christ_Pantocrator_mosaic_from_Hagia_Sophia_2744_x_2900_pixels_3.1_MB.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Christ_Pantocrator" title="Christ Pantocrator">Christ Pantocrator</a> <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">mosaic</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a></div></div></div>
<p>Most Greeks are <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christians</a>, belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church" title="Greek Orthodox Church">Greek Orthodox Church</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-abs_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-abs-199">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> During the first centuries after <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus Christ</a>, the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> was originally written in <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Koine Greek</a>, which remains the <a href="/wiki/Sacred_language" title="Sacred language">liturgical language</a> of the Greek Orthodox Church, and most of the early Christians and Church Fathers were Greek-speaking.<sup id="cite_ref-Christianity-Hellenism_190-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christianity-Hellenism-190">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> There are small groups of ethnic Greeks adhering to other <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> denominations like <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism_in_Greece" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholicism in Greece">Greek Catholics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_Evangelical_Church" title="Greek Evangelical Church">Greek Evangelicals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Free_Apostolic_Church_of_Pentecost" title="Free Apostolic Church of Pentecost">Pentecostals</a>, and groups adhering to other religions including <a href="/wiki/Romaniotes" class="mw-redirect" title="Romaniotes">Romaniot</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sephardic_Jews" class="mw-redirect" title="Sephardic Jews">Sephardic Jews</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_Muslims" title="Greek Muslims">Greek Muslims</a>. About 2,000 Greeks are members of <a href="/wiki/Hellenic_Polytheistic_Reconstructionism" class="mw-redirect" title="Hellenic Polytheistic Reconstructionism">Hellenic Polytheistic Reconstructionism</a> congregations.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Greek-speaking Muslims live mainly outside Greece in the contemporary era. There are both Christian and Muslim Greek-speaking communities in <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Lebanon" title="Greeks in Lebanon">Lebanon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Syria" title="Greeks in Syria">Syria</a>, while in the <a href="/wiki/Pontus_(region)" title="Pontus (region)">Pontus</a> region of <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> there is a large community of indeterminate size who were spared from the <a href="/wiki/Population_exchange_between_Greece_and_Turkey" title="Population exchange between Greece and Turkey">population exchange</a> because of their religious affiliation.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arts">Arts</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Greek_art" title="Greek art">Greek art</a>, <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Greece" title="Music of Greece">Music of Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_architecture" title="Ancient Greek architecture">Ancient Greek architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_theatre" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Greek theatre">Ancient Greek theatre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek_theatre" title="Modern Greek theatre">Modern Greek theatre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Greece" title="Cinema of Greece">Cinema of Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek_architecture" title="Modern Greek architecture">Modern Greek architecture</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek_literature" title="Modern Greek literature">Modern Greek literature</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhist_art" title="Greco-Buddhist art">Greco-Buddhist art</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Maria_Callas_1958.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Maria_Callas_1958.jpg/180px-Maria_Callas_1958.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Maria_Callas_1958.jpg/270px-Maria_Callas_1958.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Maria_Callas_1958.jpg/360px-Maria_Callas_1958.jpg 2x" data-file-width="581" data-file-height="759" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Maria_Callas_1958.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Renowned Greek soprano <a href="/wiki/Maria_Callas" title="Maria Callas">Maria Callas</a></div></div></div>
<p>Greek art has a long and varied history. Greeks have contributed to the visual, literary and performing arts.<sup id="cite_ref-Osborne_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osborne-204">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> In the West, <a href="/wiki/Art_in_ancient_Greece" class="mw-redirect" title="Art in ancient Greece">classical Greek art</a> was influential in shaping the <a href="/wiki/Roman_art" title="Roman art">Roman</a> and later the modern <a href="/wiki/Western_art_history" class="mw-redirect" title="Western art history">Western artistic heritage</a>. Following the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, the humanist aesthetic and the high technical standards of Greek art inspired generations of European artists.<sup id="cite_ref-Osborne_204-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osborne-204">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> Well into the 19th century, the classical tradition derived from Greece played an important role in the art of the Western world.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> In the East, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>'s conquests initiated several centuries of exchange between Greek, <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_India" title="Culture of India">Indian</a> cultures, resulting in <a href="/wiki/Indo-Greek_art" title="Indo-Greek art">Indo-Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhist_art" title="Greco-Buddhist art">Greco-Buddhist art</a>, whose influence reached as far as <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Byzantine_art" title="Byzantine art">Byzantine Greek art</a>, which grew from <a href="/wiki/Fayum_portraits" class="mw-redirect" title="Fayum portraits">classical art</a> and adapted the pagan motifs in the service of Christianity, provided a stimulus to the art of many nations.<sup id="cite_ref-Mango-Art_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mango-Art-207">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> Its influences can be traced from <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a> in the West to <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a> in the East.<sup id="cite_ref-Mango-Art_207-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mango-Art-207">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> In turn, Greek art was influenced by eastern civilizations (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Art_of_ancient_Egypt" title="Art of ancient Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persian_art" title="Persian art">Persia</a>, etc.) during various periods of its history.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Notable modern Greek artists include the major <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> painter <a href="/wiki/Dominikos_Theotokopoulos" class="mw-redirect" title="Dominikos Theotokopoulos">Dominikos Theotokopoulos</a> (El Greco), <a href="/wiki/Nikolaos_Gyzis" title="Nikolaos Gyzis">Nikolaos Gyzis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nikiphoros_Lytras" class="mw-redirect" title="Nikiphoros Lytras">Nikiphoros Lytras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Konstantinos_Volanakis" title="Konstantinos Volanakis">Konstantinos Volanakis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theodoros_Vryzakis" title="Theodoros Vryzakis">Theodoros Vryzakis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgios_Jakobides" title="Georgios Jakobides">Georgios Jakobides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thalia_Flora-Karavia" title="Thalia Flora-Karavia">Thalia Flora-Karavia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yannis_Tsarouchis" title="Yannis Tsarouchis">Yannis Tsarouchis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nikos_Engonopoulos" title="Nikos Engonopoulos">Nikos Engonopoulos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nikos_Hadjikyriakos-Ghikas" title="Nikos Hadjikyriakos-Ghikas">Nikos Hadjikyriakos-Ghikas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theofilos_Hatzimichail" class="mw-redirect" title="Theofilos Hatzimichail">Theophilos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Constantine_Andreou" title="Constantine Andreou">Kostas Andreou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jannis_Kounellis" title="Jannis Kounellis">Jannis Kounellis</a>, sculptors such as <a href="/wiki/Leonidas_Drosis" title="Leonidas Drosis">Leonidas Drosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgios_Bonanos" title="Georgios Bonanos">Georgios Bonanos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yannoulis_Chalepas" title="Yannoulis Chalepas">Yannoulis Chalepas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Athanase_Apartis" title="Athanase Apartis">Athanasios Apartis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Konstantinos_Dimitriadis" title="Konstantinos Dimitriadis">Konstantinos Dimitriadis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joannis_Avramidis" title="Joannis Avramidis">Joannis Avramidis</a>, conductor <a href="/wiki/Dimitri_Mitropoulos" title="Dimitri Mitropoulos">Dimitri Mitropoulos</a>, soprano <a href="/wiki/Maria_Callas" title="Maria Callas">Maria Callas</a>, composers such as <a href="/wiki/Mikis_Theodorakis" title="Mikis Theodorakis">Mikis Theodorakis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nikos_Skalkottas" title="Nikos Skalkottas">Nikos Skalkottas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nikolaos_Mantzaros" title="Nikolaos Mantzaros">Nikolaos Mantzaros</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spyridon_Samaras" title="Spyridon Samaras">Spyridon Samaras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manolis_Kalomiris" title="Manolis Kalomiris">Manolis Kalomiris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iannis_Xenakis" title="Iannis Xenakis">Iannis Xenakis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manos_Hatzidakis" title="Manos Hatzidakis">Manos Hatzidakis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manos_Lo%C3%AFzos" title="Manos Loïzos">Manos Loïzos</a>,  <a href="/wiki/Yanni" title="Yanni">Yanni</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vangelis" title="Vangelis">Vangelis</a>, the masters of <a href="/wiki/Rebetiko" title="Rebetiko">rebetiko</a> <a href="/wiki/Markos_Vamvakaris" title="Markos Vamvakaris">Markos Vamvakaris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vassilis_Tsitsanis" title="Vassilis Tsitsanis">Vassilis Tsitsanis</a>, and singers such as <a href="/wiki/Giorgos_Dalaras" class="mw-redirect" title="Giorgos Dalaras">Giorgos Dalaras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haris_Alexiou" title="Haris Alexiou">Haris Alexiou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sotiria_Bellou" title="Sotiria Bellou">Sotiria Bellou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nana_Mouskouri" title="Nana Mouskouri">Nana Mouskouri</a> and <a href="/wiki/Demis_Roussos" title="Demis Roussos">Demis Roussos</a>. Poets such as <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Kalvos" title="Andreas Kalvos">Andreas Kalvos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Athanasios_Christopoulos" title="Athanasios Christopoulos">Athanasios Christopoulos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kostis_Palamas" title="Kostis Palamas">Kostis Palamas</a>, the writer of <a href="/wiki/Hymn_to_Liberty" title="Hymn to Liberty">Hymn to Liberty</a> <a href="/wiki/Dionysios_Solomos" title="Dionysios Solomos">Dionysios Solomos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Angelos_Sikelianos" title="Angelos Sikelianos">Angelos Sikelianos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kostas_Karyotakis" title="Kostas Karyotakis">Kostas Karyotakis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maria_Polydouri" title="Maria Polydouri">Maria Polydouri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yannis_Ritsos" class="mw-redirect" title="Yannis Ritsos">Yannis Ritsos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kostas_Varnalis" title="Kostas Varnalis">Kostas Varnalis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nikos_Kavvadias" title="Nikos Kavvadias">Nikos Kavvadias</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Embirikos" title="Andreas Embirikos">Andreas Embirikos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kiki_Dimoula" title="Kiki Dimoula">Kiki Dimoula</a>. <a href="/wiki/Constantine_P._Cavafy" title="Constantine P. Cavafy">Constantine P. Cavafy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature" title="Nobel Prize in Literature">Nobel laureates</a> <a href="/wiki/Giorgos_Seferis" title="Giorgos Seferis">Giorgos Seferis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Odysseas_Elytis" title="Odysseas Elytis">Odysseas Elytis</a> are among the most important poets of the 20th century. Novel is also represented by <a href="/wiki/Alexandros_Papadiamantis" title="Alexandros Papadiamantis">Alexandros Papadiamantis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emmanuel_Rhoides" title="Emmanuel Rhoides">Emmanuel Rhoides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nikos_Kazantzakis" title="Nikos Kazantzakis">Nikos Kazantzakis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Penelope_Delta" title="Penelope Delta">Penelope Delta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stratis_Myrivilis" title="Stratis Myrivilis">Stratis Myrivilis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vassilis_Vassilikos" title="Vassilis Vassilikos">Vassilis Vassilikos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Petros_Markaris" title="Petros Markaris">Petros Markaris</a>, while notable playwrights include the <a href="/wiki/Cretan_Renaissance" class="mw-redirect" title="Cretan Renaissance">Cretan Renaissance</a> poets <a href="/wiki/Georgios_Chortatzis" title="Georgios Chortatzis">Georgios Chortatzis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vitsentzos_Kornaros" title="Vitsentzos Kornaros">Vincenzos Cornaros</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Gregorios_Xenopoulos" title="Gregorios Xenopoulos">Gregorios Xenopoulos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iakovos_Kambanellis" title="Iakovos Kambanellis">Iakovos Kambanellis</a>.
</p><p>Notable Greek actors include <a href="/wiki/Marika_Kotopouli" title="Marika Kotopouli">Marika Kotopouli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Melina_Mercouri" title="Melina Mercouri">Melina Mercouri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ellie_Lambeti" title="Ellie Lambeti">Ellie Lambeti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="Academy Award">Academy Award</a> winner <a href="/wiki/Katina_Paxinou" title="Katina Paxinou">Katina Paxinou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexis_Minotis" title="Alexis Minotis">Alexis Minotis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dimitris_Horn" title="Dimitris Horn">Dimitris Horn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thanasis_Veggos" title="Thanasis Veggos">Thanasis Veggos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manos_Katrakis" title="Manos Katrakis">Manos Katrakis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Irene_Papas" title="Irene Papas">Irene Papas</a>. <a href="/wiki/Alekos_Sakellarios" title="Alekos Sakellarios">Alekos Sakellarios</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karolos_Koun" title="Karolos Koun">Karolos Koun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vasilis_Georgiadis" title="Vasilis Georgiadis">Vasilis Georgiadis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Costa-Gavras" title="Costa-Gavras">Kostas Gavras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Cacoyannis" title="Michael Cacoyannis">Michael Cacoyannis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Giannis_Dalianidis" title="Giannis Dalianidis">Giannis Dalianidis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nikos_Koundouros" title="Nikos Koundouros">Nikos Koundouros</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theo_Angelopoulos" title="Theo Angelopoulos">Theo Angelopoulos</a> are among the most important directors.
</p><p>Among the most significant modern-era architects are <a href="/wiki/Stamatios_Kleanthis" title="Stamatios Kleanthis">Stamatios Kleanthis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lysandros_Kaftanzoglou" title="Lysandros Kaftanzoglou">Lysandros Kaftanzoglou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anastasios_Metaxas" title="Anastasios Metaxas">Anastasios Metaxas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Panagis_Kalkos" title="Panagis Kalkos">Panagis Kalkos</a>, the naturalized Greek <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Ziller" title="Ernst Ziller">Ernst Ziller</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dimitris_Pikionis" title="Dimitris Pikionis">Dimitris Pikionis</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Candilis" class="mw-redirect" title="George Candilis">George Candilis</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Science">Science</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">Ancient Greek philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_mathematics" title="Greek mathematics">Greek mathematics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_medicine" title="Ancient Greek medicine">Ancient Greek medicine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_science" title="Byzantine science">Byzantine science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_scholars_in_the_Renaissance" title="Greek scholars in the Renaissance">Greek scholars in the Renaissance</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Greek_inventions_and_discoveries" title="List of Greek inventions and discoveries">List of Greek inventions and discoveries</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aristarchus_working.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Aristarchus_working.jpg/220px-Aristarchus_working.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Aristarchus_working.jpg/330px-Aristarchus_working.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Aristarchus_working.jpg/440px-Aristarchus_working.jpg 2x" data-file-width="508" data-file-height="338" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aristarchus_working.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Aristarchus_of_Samos" title="Aristarchus of Samos">Aristarchus of Samos</a> was the first known individual to propose a <a href="/wiki/Heliocentrism" title="Heliocentrism">heliocentric system</a>, in the 3rd century BC</div></div></div>
<p>The Greeks of the Classical and Hellenistic eras made seminal contributions to science and philosophy, laying the foundations of several western scientific traditions, such as <a href="/wiki/Greek_astronomy" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek astronomy">astronomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geography" title="Geography">geography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Historiography" title="Historiography">historiography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_mathematics" title="Greek mathematics">mathematics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek medicine">medicine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_philosophy" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek philosophy">philosophy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Political_science" title="Political science">political science</a>. The scholarly tradition of the Greek academies was maintained during Roman times with several academic institutions in <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> and other centers of Greek learning, while Byzantine science was essentially a continuation of classical science.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> Greeks have a long tradition of valuing and investing in <i>paideia</i> (education).<sup id="cite_ref-Harris_81-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harris-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> <i>Paideia</i> was one of the highest societal values in the Greek and Hellenistic world while the first European institution described as a university was founded in 5th century Constantinople and operated in various incarnations until the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">city's fall</a> to the Ottomans in 1453.<sup id="cite_ref-Bump_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bump-211">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Constantinople" title="University of Constantinople">University of Constantinople</a> was Christian Europe's first secular institution of higher learning since no theological subjects were taught,<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> and considering the original meaning of the world university as a corporation of students, the world's first university as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Bump_211-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bump-211">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2007, Greece had the eighth highest percentage of tertiary enrollment in the world (with the percentages for female students being higher than for male) while Greeks of the Diaspora are equally active in the field of education.<sup id="cite_ref-EconWorld_169-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EconWorld-169">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> Hundreds of thousands of Greek students attend western universities every year while the faculty lists of leading Western universities contain a striking number of Greek names.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> Notable Greek scientists of modern times include Indologist <a href="/wiki/Dimitrios_Galanos" title="Dimitrios Galanos">Dimitrios Galanos</a>, Dr <a href="/wiki/Georgios_Papanikolaou" title="Georgios Papanikolaou">Georgios Papanicolaou</a> (inventor of the <a href="/wiki/Pap_test" title="Pap test">Pap test</a>), mathematician <a href="/wiki/Constantin_Carath%C3%A9odory" title="Constantin Carathéodory">Constantin Carathéodory</a>, archaeologists <a href="/wiki/Manolis_Andronikos" title="Manolis Andronikos">Manolis Andronikos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Valerios_Stais" title="Valerios Stais">Valerios Stais</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spyridon_Marinatos" title="Spyridon Marinatos">Spyridon Marinatos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ioannis_Svoronos" title="Ioannis Svoronos">Ioannis Svoronos</a>, computer scientists <a href="/wiki/Michael_Dertouzos" title="Michael Dertouzos">Michael Dertouzos</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Argyris" title="John Argyris">John Argyris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Negroponte" title="Nicholas Negroponte">Nicholas Negroponte</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Sifakis" title="Joseph Sifakis">Joseph Sifakis</a> (2007 <a href="/wiki/Turing_Award" title="Turing Award">Turing Award</a>, the "Nobel Prize" of Computer Science), <a href="/wiki/Christos_Papadimitriou" title="Christos Papadimitriou">Christos Papadimitriou</a> (2002 <a href="/wiki/Knuth_Prize" title="Knuth Prize">Knuth Prize</a>, 2012 <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6del_Prize" title="Gödel Prize">Gödel Prize</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Mihalis_Yannakakis" title="Mihalis Yannakakis">Mihalis Yannakakis</a> (2005 <a href="/wiki/Knuth_Prize" title="Knuth Prize">Knuth Prize</a>), physicists <a href="/wiki/Achilles_Papapetrou" title="Achilles Papapetrou">Achilles Papapetrou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dimitri_Nanopoulos" title="Dimitri Nanopoulos">Dimitri Nanopoulos</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Iliopoulos" title="John Iliopoulos">John Iliopoulos</a> (2007 <a href="/wiki/Dirac_Prize" class="mw-redirect" title="Dirac Prize">Dirac Prize</a> for his contributions on the physics of the charm quark, a major contribution to the birth of the Standard Model, the modern theory of Elementary Particles), astronomer <a href="/wiki/E._M._Antoniadi" title="E. M. Antoniadi">Eugenios Antoniadis</a>, biologist <a href="/wiki/Fotis_Kafatos" title="Fotis Kafatos">Fotis Kafatos</a>, botanist <a href="/wiki/Theodoros_G._Orphanides" title="Theodoros G. Orphanides">Theodoros Orphanides</a>, economist <a href="/wiki/Xenophon_Zolotas" title="Xenophon Zolotas">Xenophon Zolotas</a>, linguist <a href="/wiki/Ioannis_Psycharis" title="Ioannis Psycharis">Ioannis Psycharis</a>, historians <a href="/wiki/Constantine_Paparrigopoulos" title="Constantine Paparrigopoulos">Constantine Paparrigopoulos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helene_Ahrweiler" title="Helene Ahrweiler">Helene Glykatzi Ahrweiler</a> and political scientists <a href="/wiki/Nicos_Poulantzas" title="Nicos Poulantzas">Nicos Poulantzas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cornelius_Castoriadis" title="Cornelius Castoriadis">Cornelius Castoriadis</a>.
</p><p>Significant engineers and automobile designers include <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Tombazis" title="Nicholas Tombazis">Nicholas Tombazis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alec_Issigonis" title="Alec Issigonis">Alec Issigonis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Zapatinas" title="Andreas Zapatinas">Andreas Zapatinas</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbols">Symbols</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Greece" title="Flag of Greece">Flag of Greece</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_Greek_Orthodox_Church.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Flag_of_the_Greek_Orthodox_Church.svg/180px-Flag_of_the_Greek_Orthodox_Church.svg.png" decoding="async" width="180" height="120" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Flag_of_the_Greek_Orthodox_Church.svg/270px-Flag_of_the_Greek_Orthodox_Church.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Flag_of_the_Greek_Orthodox_Church.svg/360px-Flag_of_the_Greek_Orthodox_Church.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_Greek_Orthodox_Church.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The flag of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church" title="Greek Orthodox Church">Greek Orthodox Church</a> is based on the coat of arms of the <a href="/wiki/Palaiologoi" class="mw-redirect" title="Palaiologoi">Palaiologoi</a>, the last dynasty of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Greek_Independence_1821.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Greek_Independence_1821.svg/180px-Greek_Independence_1821.svg.png" decoding="async" width="180" height="120" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Greek_Independence_1821.svg/270px-Greek_Independence_1821.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Greek_Independence_1821.svg/360px-Greek_Independence_1821.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Greek_Independence_1821.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Traditional Greek flag up to the early stages of the Greek War of Independence.</div></div></div>
<p>The most widely used symbol is the <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Greece" title="Flag of Greece">flag of Greece</a>, which features nine equal horizontal stripes of blue alternating with white representing the nine syllables of the Greek national motto <i><a href="/wiki/Eleftheria_i_Thanatos" class="mw-redirect" title="Eleftheria i Thanatos">Eleftheria i Thanatos</a></i> (Freedom or Death), which was the motto of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_War_of_Independence" title="Greek War of Independence">Greek War of Independence</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> The blue square in the upper hoist-side corner bears a white cross, which represents <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church" title="Greek Orthodox Church">Greek Orthodoxy</a>. The Greek flag is widely used by the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Cypriots" title="Greek Cypriots">Greek Cypriots</a>, although <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> has officially adopted a neutral flag to ease ethnic tensions with the <a href="/wiki/Turkish_Cypriots" title="Turkish Cypriots">Turkish Cypriot</a> minority (see <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Cyprus" title="Flag of Cyprus">flag of Cyprus</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The pre-1978 (and first) flag of Greece, which features a <a href="/wiki/Cross" title="Cross">Greek cross</a> (<i>crux immissa quadrata</i>) on a blue background, is widely used as an alternative to the official flag, and they are often flown together. The <a href="/wiki/National_emblem_of_Greece" class="mw-redirect" title="National emblem of Greece">national emblem of Greece</a> features a blue <a href="/wiki/Escutcheon_(heraldry)" title="Escutcheon (heraldry)">escutcheon</a> with a white cross surrounded by two laurel branches. A common design involves the current flag of Greece and the pre-1978 flag of Greece with crossed flagpoles and the national emblem placed in front.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another highly recognizable and popular Greek symbol is the <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Greece#Double-headed_eagle" title="Flag of Greece">double-headed eagle</a>, the imperial emblem of the last dynasty of the Eastern Roman Empire and a common symbol in <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> and, later, <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> It is not part of the modern Greek flag or coat-of-arms, although it is officially the insignia of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Army" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Army">Greek Army</a> and the flag of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Greece" title="Church of Greece">Church of Greece</a>. It had been incorporated in the Greek coat of arms between 1925 and 1926.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Politics_in_Greece" class="mw-redirect" title="Politics in Greece">Politics in Greece</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Classical_Athens" title="Classical Athens">Classical Athens</a> is considered the birthplace of <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">Democracy</a>. The term appeared in the 5th century BC to denote the political systems then existing in <a href="/wiki/Greek_city-state" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek city-state">Greek city-states</a>, notably Athens, to mean "rule of the people", in contrast to <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy" title="Aristocracy">aristocracy</a> (<span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">ἀριστοκρατία</span>, <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">aristokratía</i></i>), meaning "rule by an excellent elite", and to <a href="/wiki/Oligarchy" title="Oligarchy">oligarchy</a>. While theoretically these definitions are in opposition, in practice the distinction has been blurred historically.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> Led by <a href="/wiki/Cleisthenes" title="Cleisthenes">Cleisthenes</a>, Athenians established what is generally held as the first democracy in 508–507 BC <sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup>, which took gradually the form of a <a href="/wiki/Direct_democracy" title="Direct democracy">direct democracy</a>. The democratic form of government declined during the Hellenistic and Roman eras, only to be revived as an interest in Western Europe during the <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_period" title="Early modern period">early modern period</a>.
</p><p>The European enlightenment and the democratic, liberal and nationalistic ideas of the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> was a crucial factor to the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_War_of_Independence" title="Greek War of Independence">Greek War of Independence</a> and the establishment of the modern Greek state.<sup id="cite_ref-Cl48_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cl48-221">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Paroulakis32_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paroulakis32-222">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>Notable modern Greek politicians include <a href="/wiki/Ioannis_Kapodistrias" title="Ioannis Kapodistrias">Ioannis Kapodistrias</a>, founder of the <a href="/wiki/First_Hellenic_Republic" title="First Hellenic Republic">First Hellenic Republic</a>, reformist <a href="/wiki/Charilaos_Trikoupis" title="Charilaos Trikoupis">Charilaos Trikoupis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eleftherios_Venizelos" title="Eleftherios Venizelos">Eleftherios Venizelos</a>, who marked the shape of modern Greece, <a href="/wiki/Konstantinos_Karamanlis" title="Konstantinos Karamanlis">Konstantinos Karamanlis</a>, founder of the <a href="/wiki/Third_Hellenic_Republic" title="Third Hellenic Republic">Third Hellenic Republic</a>, and socialist <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Papandreou" title="Andreas Papandreou">Andreas Papandreou</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surnames_and_personal_names">Surnames and personal names</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Greek_name" title="Greek name">Greek name</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_personal_names" title="Ancient Greek personal names">Ancient Greek personal names</a></div>
<p>Greek surnames began to appear in the 9th and 10th century, at first among ruling families, eventually supplanting the ancient tradition of using the father's name as disambiguator.<sup id="cite_ref-Wickham_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickham-223">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lexicon_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lexicon-224">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, Greek surnames are most commonly patronymics,<sup id="cite_ref-Wickham_223-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wickham-223">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> such those ending in the suffix <i>-opoulos</i> or <i>-ides</i>, while others derive from trade professions, physical characteristics, or a location such as a town, village, or monastery.<sup id="cite_ref-lexicon_224-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lexicon-224">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> Commonly, Greek male surnames end in -s, which is the common ending for Greek masculine <a href="/wiki/Proper_nouns" class="mw-redirect" title="Proper nouns">proper nouns</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Nominative_case" title="Nominative case">nominative case</a>. Occasionally (especially in Cyprus), some surnames end in <i>-ou</i>, indicating the <a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive case</a> of a patronymic name.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> Many surnames end in suffixes that are associated with a particular region, such as <i>-akis</i> (Crete), <i>-eas</i> or <i>-akos</i> (<a href="/wiki/Mani_Peninsula" title="Mani Peninsula">Mani Peninsula</a>), <i>-atos</i> (island of <a href="/wiki/Cephalonia" title="Cephalonia">Cephalonia</a>), <i>-ellis</i> (island of <a href="/wiki/Lesbos" title="Lesbos">Lesbos</a>) and so forth.<sup id="cite_ref-lexicon_224-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lexicon-224">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> In addition to a Greek origin, some surnames have Turkish or Latin/Italian origin, especially among Greeks from <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ionian_Islands" title="Ionian Islands">Ionian Islands</a>, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> Female surnames end in a vowel and are usually the genitive form of the corresponding males surname, although this usage is not followed in the diaspora, where the male version of the surname is generally used.
</p><p>With respect to personal names, the two main influences are Christianity and classical Hellenism; ancient Greek nomenclatures were never forgotten but have become more widely bestowed from the 18th century onwards.<sup id="cite_ref-lexicon_224-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lexicon-224">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> As in antiquity, children are customarily named after their grandparents, with the first born male child named after the paternal grandfather, the second male child after the maternal grandfather, and similarly for female children.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> Personal names are often familiarized by a diminutive suffix, such as <i>-akis</i> for male names and <i>-itsa</i> or <i>-oula</i> for female names.<sup id="cite_ref-lexicon_224-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lexicon-224">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> Greeks generally do not use middle names, instead using the genitive of the father's first name as a middle name. This usage has been passed on to the <a href="/wiki/Russian_names" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian names">Russians</a> and other <a href="/wiki/East_Slavs" title="East Slavs">East Slavs</a> (<a href="/wiki/Otchestvo" class="mw-redirect" title="Otchestvo">otchestvo</a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sea:_exploring_and_commerce">Sea: exploring and commerce</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Greek_shipping" title="Greek shipping">Greek shipping</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aristotle_Onassis_1967cr.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Aristotle_Onassis_1967cr.jpg/150px-Aristotle_Onassis_1967cr.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Aristotle_Onassis_1967cr.jpg/225px-Aristotle_Onassis_1967cr.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Aristotle_Onassis_1967cr.jpg/300px-Aristotle_Onassis_1967cr.jpg 2x" data-file-width="914" data-file-height="1218" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aristotle_Onassis_1967cr.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Aristotle_Onassis" title="Aristotle Onassis">Aristotle Onassis</a>, the best known Greek shipping magnate worldwide.</div></div></div>
<p>The traditional Greek homelands have been the Greek peninsula and the Aegean Sea, <a href="/wiki/Southern_Italy" title="Southern Italy">Southern Italy</a> (<a href="/wiki/Magna_Graecia" title="Magna Graecia">Magna Graecia</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">Ionian coasts</a> of <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> and the islands of <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>. In Plato's <i><a href="/wiki/Phaedo" title="Phaedo">Phaidon</a></i>, Socrates remarks, "we (Greeks) live around a sea like frogs around a pond" when describing to his friends the Greek cities of the Aegean.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Harl_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harl-229">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> This image is attested by the map of the Old Greek Diaspora, which corresponded to the Greek world until the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greek state</a> in 1832. The <a href="/wiki/Sea" title="Sea">sea</a> and trade were natural outlets for Greeks since the Greek peninsula is mostly rocky and does not offer good prospects for agriculture.<sup id="cite_ref-Roberts1_38-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts1-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Notable Greek seafarers include people such as <a href="/wiki/Pytheas" title="Pytheas">Pytheas of Massalia</a> who sailed to Great Britain, <a href="/wiki/Euthymenes" title="Euthymenes">Euthymenes</a> who sailed to Africa, <a href="/wiki/Scylax_of_Caryanda" title="Scylax of Caryanda">Scylax of Caryanda</a> who sailed to India, the <a href="/wiki/Navarch" title="Navarch">navarch</a> of Alexander the Great <a href="/wiki/Nearchus" title="Nearchus">Nearchus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Megasthenes" title="Megasthenes">Megasthenes</a>, explorer of India, later the 6th century merchant and monk <a href="/wiki/Cosmas_Indicopleustes" title="Cosmas Indicopleustes">Cosmas Indicopleustes</a> (<i>Cosmas who sailed to India</i>), and the explorer of the Northwestern Passage Ioannis Fokas also known as <a href="/wiki/Juan_de_Fuca" title="Juan de Fuca">Juan de Fuca</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> In later times, the Byzantine Greeks plied the sea-lanes of the Mediterranean and controlled trade until an embargo imposed by the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_emperor" class="mw-redirect" title="Byzantine emperor">Byzantine emperor</a> on trade with the <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">Caliphate</a> opened the door for the later Italian pre-eminence in trade.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Panayotis_Potagos" title="Panayotis Potagos">Panayotis Potagos</a> was another explorer of modern times who was the first to reach Mbomu and <a href="/wiki/Uele_River" title="Uele River"> Uele river</a> from the north. 
</p><p>The Greek shipping tradition recovered during the late Ottoman rule (especially after the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_K%C3%BC%C3%A7%C3%BCk_Kaynarca" title="Treaty of Küçük Kaynarca">Treaty of Küçük Kaynarca</a> and during the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>), when a substantial merchant middle class developed, which played an important part in the Greek War of Independence.<sup id="cite_ref-BritIdent_109-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BritIdent-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Today, Greek shipping continues to prosper to the extent that Greece has one of the largest merchant fleets in the world, while many more ships under Greek ownership fly <a href="/wiki/Flags_of_convenience" class="mw-redirect" title="Flags of convenience">flags of convenience</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EconWorld_169-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EconWorld-169">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> The most notable shipping <a href="/wiki/Magnate" title="Magnate">magnate</a> of the 20th century was <a href="/wiki/Aristotle_Onassis" title="Aristotle Onassis">Aristotle Onassis</a>, others being <a href="/wiki/Yiannis_Latsis" title="Yiannis Latsis">Yiannis Latsis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stavros_G._Livanos" title="Stavros G. Livanos">Stavros G. Livanos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Stavros_Niarchos" title="Stavros Niarchos">Stavros Niarchos</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Genetic_history_of_Europe" title="Genetic history of Europe">Genetic history of Europe</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Plos.Balkans.2.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Plos.Balkans.2.png/200px-Plos.Balkans.2.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="78" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Plos.Balkans.2.png/300px-Plos.Balkans.2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Plos.Balkans.2.png/400px-Plos.Balkans.2.png 2x" data-file-width="4033" data-file-height="1581" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Plos.Balkans.2.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Admixture analysis of <a href="/wiki/Autosomal" class="mw-redirect" title="Autosomal">autosomal</a> <a href="/wiki/Single-nucleotide_polymorphism" title="Single-nucleotide polymorphism">SNPs</a> of the Balkan region in a global context on the resolution level of 7 assumed ancestral populations: African (brown), South/West European (light blue), Asian (yellow), Middle Eastern (green), North/East European (dark blue) and Caucasian/Anatolian component (beige).</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:European_population_substructure.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/European_population_substructure.png/180px-European_population_substructure.png" decoding="async" width="180" height="129" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/European_population_substructure.png/270px-European_population_substructure.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/European_population_substructure.png/360px-European_population_substructure.png 2x" data-file-width="1792" data-file-height="1280" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:European_population_substructure.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Factor Correspondence Analysis Comparing Different Individuals from European Ancestry Groups.</div></div></div>
<p>Genetic studies using <a href="/wiki/Genetic_marker" title="Genetic marker">multiple autosomal gene markers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Y_DNA" class="mw-redirect" title="Y DNA">Y chromosomal DNA</a> <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup" title="Haplogroup">haplogroup</a> analysis and <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial gene markers</a> (mtDNA) show that Greeks share similar backgrounds as the rest of the Europeans and especially southern Europeans (<a href="/wiki/Italians" title="Italians">Italians</a> and southern <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkan</a> populations). According to the studies using multiple autosomal gene markers, Greeks are some of the earliest contributors of genetic material to the rest of the Europeans as they are one of the oldest populations in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Cavalli-Sforza_1996_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cavalli-Sforza_1996-234">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> A study in 2008 showed that Greeks are genetically closest to <a href="/wiki/Italians" title="Italians">Italians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Romanians" title="Romanians">Romanians</a><sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> and another 2008 study showed that they are close to Italians, <a href="/wiki/Albanians" title="Albanians">Albanians</a>, Romanians and <a href="/wiki/South_Slavs" title="South Slavs">southern Balkan Slavs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Novembre_et_al_2008_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Novembre_et_al_2008-236">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> A 2003 study showed that Greeks cluster with other South European (mainly Italians) and North-European populations and are close to the <a href="/wiki/Basques" title="Basques">Basques</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Fixation_index" title="Fixation index">F<sub>ST</sub> distances</a> showed that they group with other European and Mediterranean populations,<sup id="cite_ref-Cavalli-Sforza_1996_234-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cavalli-Sforza_1996-234">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> especially with Italians (−0.0001) and Tuscans (0.0005).<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup>
