</p><p>Y DNA studies show that Greeks cluster with other Europeans<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> and that they carry some of the oldest <a href="/wiki/Human_Y-chromosome_DNA_haplogroup" title="Human Y-chromosome DNA haplogroup">Y haplogroups</a> in Europe, in particular the <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_J-M172" title="Haplogroup J-M172">J2 haplogroup</a> (and other J subhaplogroups) and <a href="/wiki/Y-DNA_haplogroups_by_ethnic_group" title="Y-DNA haplogroups by ethnic group">E haplogroups</a>, which are genetic markers denoting early farmers.<sup id="cite_ref-jogg.info_240-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jogg.info-240">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Semino_et_al_2004_244-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Semino_et_al_2004-244">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Di_Gaetano_et_al_2009_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Di_Gaetano_et_al_2009-246">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> The Y-chromosome lineage <a href="/wiki/E-V13" class="mw-redirect" title="E-V13">E-V13</a> appears to have originated in Greece or the southern Balkans and is high in Greeks as well as in Albanians, southern Italians and southern Slavs. E-V13 is also found in <a href="/wiki/Corsica" title="Corsica">Corsicans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Provence" title="Provence">Provencals</a>, where an admixture analysis estimated that 17% of the Y-chromosomes of Provence may be attributed to <a href="/wiki/Colonies_in_antiquity#Greek_colonies" title="Colonies in antiquity">Greek colonization</a>, and is also found at low frequencies on the <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolian</a> mainland. These results suggest that E-V13 may trace the demographic and socio-cultural impact of Greek colonization in Mediterranean Europe, a contribution that appears to be considerably larger than that of a Neolithic pioneer colonization.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> A study in 2008 showed that Greek regional samples from the mainland cluster with those from the Balkans while <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Cretan</a> Greeks cluster with the central Mediterranean and Eastern Mediterranean samples.<sup id="cite_ref-King_et_al_2008_241-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-King_et_al_2008-241">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> Greek signature DNA influence can be seen in Southern Italy and <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>, where the genetic contribution of Greek chromosomes to the Sicilian gene pool is estimated to be about 37%, and the Southern Balkans.<sup id="cite_ref-Semino_et_al_2004_244-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Semino_et_al_2004-244">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Di_Gaetano_et_al_2009_246-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Di_Gaetano_et_al_2009-246">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Studies using mitochondrial DNA gene markers (mtDNA) showed that Greeks group with other Mediterranean European populations<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup>  and <a href="/wiki/Principal_component_analysis" title="Principal component analysis">principal component analysis</a> (PCA) confirmed the low genetic distance between Greeks and Italians<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> and also revealed a cline of genes with highest frequencies in the Balkans and Southern Italy, spreading to lowest levels in Britain and the Basque country, which <a href="/wiki/Luigi_Luca_Cavalli-Sforza" title="Luigi Luca Cavalli-Sforza">Cavalli-Sforza</a> associates it with "the Greek expansion, which reached its peak in historical times around 1000 and 500 BC but which certainly began earlier".<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2017 study on the genetic origins of the <a href="/wiki/Minoans" class="mw-redirect" title="Minoans">Minoans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greece" title="Mycenaean Greece">Mycenaeans</a> showed that modern Greeks resemble the Mycenaeans, but with some additional dilution of the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">early neolithic</a> ancestry. The results of the study support the idea of genetic continuity between these civilizations and modern Greeks but not isolation in the history of populations of the Aegean, before and after the time of its earliest civilizations.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> In an interview, the study's author, <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a> geneticist Iosif Lazaridis, precised "that all three <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> groups (Minoans, Mycenaeans, and Bronze Age southwestern <a href="/wiki/Anatolians" class="mw-redirect" title="Anatolians">Anatolians</a>) trace most of their ancestry from the earlier Neolithic populations that were very similar in Greece and western Anatolia. But, they also had some ancestry from the 'east', related to populations of the <a href="/wiki/Peoples_of_the_Caucasus" title="Peoples of the Caucasus">Caucasus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iranic_peoples" class="mw-redirect" title="Iranic peoples">Iran</a>" as well as "some ancestry from the "north", related to hunter-gatherers of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">eastern Europe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a> and also to the Bronze Age <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_nomads" title="Eurasian nomads">people of the steppe</a>", and argues that "some had theorized that the Minoan and Mycenaean civilizations were influenced both culturally and genetically by the old civilizations of the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egypt</a>, but there is no quantifiable genetic influence".<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_appearance">Physical appearance</span></h2>
<p>A study from 2013 for prediction of hair and eye colour from DNA of the Greek people showed that the self-reported phenotype frequencies according to hair and eye colour categories was as follows: 119 individuals – hair colour, 11 <a href="/wiki/Blond" title="Blond">blond</a>, 45 dark blond/light brown, 49 dark brown, 3 brown red/auburn and 11 had black hair; eye colour, 13 with <a href="/wiki/Blue" title="Blue">blue</a>, 15 with intermediate (green, heterochromia) and 91 had brown eye colour.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another study from 2012 included 150 dental school students from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Athens" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Athens">University of Athens</a>, and the results of the study showed that light hair colour (blonde/light ash brown) was predominant in 10.7% of the students. 36% had medium hair colour (light brown/medium darkest brown), 32% had darkest brown and 21% black (15.3 off black, 6% midnight black). In conclusion, the hair colour of young Greeks are mostly brown, ranging from light to dark brown with significant minorities having black and blonde hair. The same study also showed that the eye colour of the students was 14.6% blue/green, 28% medium (light brown) and 57.4% dark brown.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Timeline">Timeline</span></h2>
<p>The history of the Greek people is closely associated with the history of Greece, Cyprus, Constantinople, Asia Minor and the Black Sea. During the Ottoman rule of Greece, a number of Greek enclaves around the Mediterranean were cut off from the core, notably in Southern Italy, the Caucasus, Syria and Egypt. By the early 20th century, over half of the overall <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>-speaking population was settled in Asia Minor (now Turkey), while later that century a huge wave of migration to the United States, Australia, Canada and elsewhere created the modern Greek diaspora.
</p>
<div>

<td><a href="/wiki/Proto-Greek_language" title="Proto-Greek language">Proto-Greek</a> tribes from around the Southern Balkans/Aegean are generally thought to have arrived in the Greek mainland.
</td></tr>

<td><b>16th century BC</b></td>
<td>Decline of the <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">Minoan civilization</a>, possibly because of the <a href="/wiki/Minoan_eruption" title="Minoan eruption">eruption of Thera</a>. Emergence of the <a href="/wiki/Achaeans_(tribe)" title="Achaeans (tribe)">Achaeans</a> and formation of the <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greece" title="Mycenaean Greece">Mycenaean civilization</a>, the first Greek-speaking civilization.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>13th century BC</b></td>
<td>First <a href="/wiki/Colonies_in_antiquity" title="Colonies in antiquity">colonies</a> established in <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>11th century BC</b></td>
<td>The Mycenaean civilization ends in the presumed <a href="/wiki/Dorian_invasion" title="Dorian invasion">Dorian invasion</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Greek_Dark_Ages" title="Greek Dark Ages">Greek Dark Ages</a> begin. <a href="/wiki/Dorians" title="Dorians">Dorians</a> move into peninsular <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>. Achaeans flee to <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Islands" title="Aegean Islands">Aegean Islands</a>, Asia Minor and <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>9th century BC</b></td>
<td>Major colonization of Asia Minor and Cyprus by the Greek tribes.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>8th century BC</b></td>
<td>First major colonies established in <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Italy" title="Southern Italy">Southern Italy</a>. The first Pan-Hellenic festival, the Olympic games, is held in 776 BC. The emergence of Pan-Hellenism marks the <a href="/wiki/Ethnogenesis" title="Ethnogenesis">ethnogenesis</a> of the Greek nation.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>6th century BC</b></td>
<td>Colonies established across the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>5th century BC</b></td>
<td>Defeat of the Persians and emergence of the Delian League in <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">Ionia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> and Aegean perimeter culminates in <a href="/wiki/Athenian_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Athenian Empire">Athenian Empire</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">Classical Age of Greece</a>; ends with Athens defeat by Sparta at the close of the <a href="/wiki/Peloponnesian_War" title="Peloponnesian War">Peloponnesian War</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>4th century BC</b></td>
<td>Rise of <a href="/wiki/Thebes_(Greece)" class="mw-redirect" title="Thebes (Greece)">Theban</a> power and defeat of the Spartans; <a href="/wiki/Rise_of_Macedon" class="mw-redirect" title="Rise of Macedon">Rise of Macedon</a>; Campaign of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>; Greek colonies established in newly founded cities of <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Ptolemaic Egypt">Ptolemaic Egypt</a> and Asia.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>2nd century BC</b></td>
<td>Conquest of Greece by the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>. Migrations of Greeks to <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a>.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>4th century AD</b></td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Eastern_Roman_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Roman Empire">Eastern Roman Empire</a>. Migrations of Greeks throughout the Empire, mainly towards <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>7th century</b></td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Slavic_peoples" class="mw-redirect" title="Slavic peoples">Slavic</a> conquest of several parts of <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, Greek migrations to <a href="/wiki/Southern_Italy" title="Southern Italy">Southern Italy</a>, Roman emperors capture main Slavic bodies and transfer them to <a href="/wiki/Cappadocia" title="Cappadocia">Cappadocia</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Bosphorus" class="mw-redirect" title="Bosphorus">Bosphorus</a> is re-populated by Macedonian and Cypriot Greeks.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>8th century</b></td>
<td>Roman dissolution of surviving Slavic settlements in Greece and full recovery of the Greek peninsula.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>9th century</b></td>
<td>Retro-migrations of Greeks from all parts of the Empire (mainly from Southern Italy and Sicily) into parts of Greece that were depopulated by the <a href="/wiki/Slavs" title="Slavs">Slavic Invasions</a> (mainly western Peloponnesus and Thessaly).
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>13th century</b></td>
<td>Roman Empire dissolves, Constantinople taken by the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Crusade" title="Fourth Crusade">Fourth Crusade</a>; becoming the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Latin_Empire" title="Latin Empire">Latin Empire</a>. Liberated after a long struggle by the Empire of Nicaea, but fragments remain separated. Migrations between Asia Minor, Constantinople and mainland Greece take place.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>15th century<br /><span class="nowrap">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span>–<br />19th century</b></td>
<td>Conquest of Constantinople by the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>. <a href="/wiki/Greek_diaspora" title="Greek diaspora">Greek diaspora</a> into Europe begins. Ottoman settlements in Greece. <a href="/wiki/Phanariot" class="mw-redirect" title="Phanariot">Phanariot</a> Greeks occupy high posts in Eastern European millets.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>1830s</b></td>
<td>Creation of the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Modern_Greece" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Modern Greece">Modern Greek State</a>. Immigration to the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a> begins. Large-scale migrations from Constantinople and Asia Minor to Greece take place.
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>&#32;
</p>
</td>
<td style="text-align: left; vertical-align: top;">
<table class="wikitable" style="font-size:90%;">

<tbody><tr>
<th style="width:120px">Time
</th>
<th style="width:400px">Events
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>1913</b></td>
<td>European Ottoman lands partitioned; unorganized migrations of Greeks, Bulgarians and Turks towards their respective states.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>1914–1923</b></td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Greek_genocide" title="Greek genocide">Greek genocide</a>; hundreds of thousands of <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Greeks" title="Ottoman Greeks">Ottoman Greeks</a> are estimated to have died during this period.<sup id="cite_ref-Rummel_262-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rummel-262">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>1919</b></td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Neuilly" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty of Neuilly">Treaty of Neuilly</a>; Greece and Bulgaria exchange populations, with some exceptions.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>1922</b></td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Great_Fire_of_Smyrna" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Fire of Smyrna">The Destruction of Smyrna</a> (modern-day Izmir) more than 40 thousand Greeks killed; end of significant Greek presence in Asia Minor.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>1923</b></td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Lausanne" title="Treaty of Lausanne">Treaty of Lausanne</a>; Greece and Turkey agree to exchange populations with limited exceptions of the Greeks in <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, <a href="/wiki/Imbros" title="Imbros">Imbros</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tenedos" title="Tenedos">Tenedos</a> and the Muslim minority of <a href="/wiki/Western_Thrace" title="Western Thrace">Western Thrace</a>. 1.5 million of Asia Minor and Pontic Greeks settle in Greece, and some 450 thousands of Muslims settle in Turkey.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>1940s</b></td>
<td>Hundred of thousands Greeks died from starvation during the <a href="/wiki/Axis_Occupation_of_Greece" class="mw-redirect" title="Axis Occupation of Greece">Axis Occupation of Greece</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>1947</b></td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Communist" class="mw-redirect" title="Communist">Communist</a> regime in Romania begins evictions of the Greek community, approx. 75,000 migrate.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>1948</b></td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Greek_Civil_War" title="Greek Civil War">Greek Civil War</a>. Tens of thousands of Greek <a href="/wiki/Communist" class="mw-redirect" title="Communist">communists</a> and their families flee into <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Bloc" title="Eastern Bloc">Eastern Bloc</a> nations. Thousands settle in <a href="/wiki/Tashkent" title="Tashkent">Tashkent</a>.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>1950s</b></td>
<td>Massive emigration of Greeks to <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">West Germany</a>, the United States, Australia, Canada, and other countries.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>1955</b></td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Istanbul_Pogrom" class="mw-redirect" title="Istanbul Pogrom">Istanbul Pogrom</a> against Greeks. Exodus of Greeks from the city accelerates; less than 2,000 remain today.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>1958</b></td>
<td>Large Greek community in Alexandria flees <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Abdel_Nasser" title="Gamal Abdel Nasser">Nasser's</a> regime in <a href="/wiki/History_of_Modern_Egypt#Nasser_and_Arab_socialism" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Modern Egypt">Egypt</a>.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>1960s</b></td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Cyprus" class="mw-redirect" title="Republic of Cyprus">Republic of Cyprus</a> created as an independent state under Greek, Turkish and British protection. Economic emigration continues.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>1974</b></td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Turkish_invasion_of_Cyprus" title="Turkish invasion of Cyprus">Turkish invasion of Cyprus</a>. Almost all Greeks living in Northern Cyprus flee to the south and the United Kingdom.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>1980s</b></td>
<td>Many civil war refugees were allowed to re-emigrate to Greece. Retro-migration of Greeks from Germany begins.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>1990s</b></td>
<td>Collapse of <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>. Approximately 340,000 ethnic Greeks migrate from Georgia, Armenia, southern Russia, and Albania to Greece.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>early 2000s</b></td>
<td>Some statistics show the beginning of a trend of reverse migration of Greeks from the United States and Australia.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>2010s</b></td>
<td>Over 200,000 people,<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> particularly young <a href="/wiki/Brain_drain" class="mw-redirect" title="Brain drain">skilled individuals</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> emigrate to other EU states due to high unemployment (see also <a href="/wiki/Greek_government-debt_crisis" title="Greek government-debt crisis">Greek government-debt crisis</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>&#32;
</p>
</td></tr></tbody></table></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-51">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">There is a range of interpretations: <a href="/wiki/Carl_Blegen" title="Carl Blegen">Carl Blegen</a> dates the arrival of the Greeks around 1900 BC, John Caskey believes that there were two waves of immigrants and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Drews" title="Robert Drews">Robert Drews</a> places the event as late as 1600 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Numerous other theories have also been supported,<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> but there is a general consensus that the Greek tribes arrived around 2100 BC.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-128"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-128">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">While Greek authorities signed the agreement legalizing the population exchange this was done on the insistence of <a href="/wiki/Mustafa_Kemal_Atat%C3%BCrk" title="Mustafa Kemal Atatürk">Mustafa Kemal Atatürk</a> and after a million Greeks had already been expelled from <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Asia Minor</a> (<a href="#CITEREFGilbar1997">Gilbar 1997</a>, p.&#160;8).</span>
</li>
</ol></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Citations">Citations</span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">

</li>
<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFClogg2013">Clogg 2013</a>, p.&#160;228: "Greeks of the diaspora, settled in some 141 countries, were held to number 7 million although it is not clear how this figure was arrived at or what criteria were used to define Greek ethnicity, while the population of the homeland, according to the 1991 census, amounted to some 10.25 million."</span>

<li id="cite_note-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-17">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mfa.gr/en/greece-bilateral-relations/italy/cultural-relations-and-greek-community.html">"Italy: Cultural Relations and Greek Community"</a>. <i>Hellenic Republic: Ministry of Foreign Affairs</i>. 9 July 2013. <q>The Greek Italian community numbers some 30,000 and is concentrated mainly in central Italy. The age-old presence in Italy of Italians of Greek descent – dating back to Byzantine and Classical times – is attested to by the Griko dialect, which is still spoken in the Magna Graecia region. This historically Greek-speaking villages are Condofuri, Galliciano, Roccaforte del Greco, Roghudi, Bova and Bova Marina, which are in the Calabria region (the capital of which is Reggio). The Grecanic region, including Reggio, has a population of some 200,000, while speakers of the Griko dialect number fewer that 1,000 persons.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Hellenic+Republic%3A+Ministry+of+Foreign+Affairs&amp;rft.atitle=Italy%3A+Cultural+Relations+and+Greek+Community&amp;rft.date=2013-07-09&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.mfa.gr%2Fen%2Fgreece-bilateral-relations%2Fitaly%2Fcultural-relations-and-greek-community.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGreeks" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Grecia-Salentina-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Grecia-Salentina_18-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Grecia-Salentina_18-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.greciasalentina.gov.it/">"Grecia Salentina"</a> (in Italian). Unione dei Comuni della Grecìa Salentina. 2016. <q>La popolazione complessiva dell'Unione è di 54278 residenti così distribuiti (Dati Istat al 31° dicembre 2005. Comune Popolazione Calimera 7351 Carpignano Salentino 3868 Castrignano dei Greci 4164 Corigliano d'Otranto 5762 Cutrofiano 9250 Martano 9588 Martignano 1784 Melpignano 2234 Soleto 5551 Sternatia 2583 Zollino 2143 Totale 54278).</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Grecia+Salentina&amp;rft.pub=Unione+dei+Comuni+della+Grec%C3%ACa+Salentina&amp;rft.date=2016&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.greciasalentina.gov.it%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGreeks" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-Bellinello-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Bellinello_19-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Bellinello_19-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBellinello1998">Bellinello 1998</a>, p.&#160;53: "Le attuali colonie Greche calabresi; La Grecìa calabrese si inscrive nel massiccio aspromontano e si concentra nell'ampia e frastagliata valle dell'Amendolea e nelle balze più a oriente, dove sorgono le fiumare dette di S. Pasquale, di Palizzi e Sidèroni e che costituiscono la Bovesia vera e propria. Compresa nei territori di cinque comuni (Bova Superiore, Bova Marina, Roccaforte del Greco, Roghudi, Condofuri), la Grecia si estende per circa 233&#160;kmq. La popolazione anagrafica complessiva è di circa 14.000 unità."</span>

<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mfa.gr/en/greece-bilateral-relations/south-africa/cultural-relations-and-greek-community.html">"South Africa: Cultural Relations and Greek Community"</a>. <i>Hellenic Republic: Ministry of Foreign Affairs</i>. 4 February 2011. <q>It is difficult to determine the precise number of Greeks due to constant comings and goings, although the estimated figure is above 45,000.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Hellenic+Republic%3A+Ministry+of+Foreign+Affairs&amp;rft.atitle=South+Africa%3A+Cultural+Relations+and+Greek+Community&amp;rft.date=2011-02-04&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.mfa.gr%2Fen%2Fgreece-bilateral-relations%2Fsouth-africa%2Fcultural-relations-and-greek-community.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGreeks" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-22">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mfa.gr/en/greece-bilateral-relations/france/cultural-relations-and-greek-community.html">"France: Cultural Relations and Greek Community"</a>. <i>Hellenic Republic: Ministry of Foreign Affairs</i>. 9 July 2013. <q>Some 15,000 Greeks reside in the wider region of Paris, Lille and Lyon. In the region of Southern France, the Greek community numbers some 20,000.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Hellenic+Republic%3A+Ministry+of+Foreign+Affairs&amp;rft.atitle=France%3A+Cultural+Relations+and+Greek+Community&amp;rft.date=2013-07-09&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.mfa.gr%2Fen%2Fgreece-bilateral-relations%2Ffrance%2Fcultural-relations-and-greek-community.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGreeks" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-23">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mfa.gr/en/greece-bilateral-relations/belgium/cultural-relations-and-greek-community.html">"Belgium: Cultural Relations and Greek Community"</a>. <i>Hellenic Republic: Ministry of Foreign Affairs</i>. 28 January 2011. <q>Some 35,000 Greeks reside in Belgium. Official Belgian data numbers Greeks in the country at 17,000, but does not take into account Greeks who have taken Belgian citizenship or work for international organizations and enterprises.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Hellenic+Republic%3A+Ministry+of+Foreign+Affairs&amp;rft.atitle=Belgium%3A+Cultural+Relations+and+Greek+Community&amp;rft.date=2011-01-28&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.mfa.gr%2Fen%2Fgreece-bilateral-relations%2Fbelgium%2Fcultural-relations-and-greek-community.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGreeks" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-24">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mfa.gr/en/greece-bilateral-relations/bulgaria/cultural-relations-and-greek-community.html">"Bulgaria: Cultural Relations and Greek Community"</a>. <i>Hellenic Republic: Ministry of Foreign Affairs</i>. 28 January 2011. <q>There are some 28,500 persons of Greek origin and citizenship residing in Bulgaria. This number includes approximately 15,000 Sarakatsani, 2,500 former political refugees, 8,000 "old Greeks", 2,000 university students and 1,000 professionals and their families.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Hellenic+Republic%3A+Ministry+of+Foreign+Affairs&amp;rft.atitle=Bulgaria%3A+Cultural+Relations+and+Greek+Community&amp;rft.date=2011-01-28&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.mfa.gr%2Fen%2Fgreece-bilateral-relations%2Fbulgaria%2Fcultural-relations-and-greek-community.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGreeks" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-43">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFGeorgiev1981">Georgiev 1981</a>, p. 156: "The Proto-Greek region included Epirus, approximately up to <i>Αυλών</i> in the north including Paravaia, Tymphaia, Athamania, Dolopia, Amphilochia, and Acarnania), west and north Thessaly (Hestiaiotis, Perrhaibia, Tripolis, and Pieria), i.e. more or less the territory of contemporary northwestern Greece)."</span>

<li id="cite_note-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-44">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFGuibernauHutchinson2004">Guibernau &amp; Hutchinson 2004</a>, p.&#160;23: "Indeed, Smith emphasizes that the myth of divine election sustains the continuity of cultural identity, and, in that regard, has enabled certain pre-modern communities such as the Jews, Armenians, and Greeks to survive and persist over centuries and millennia (Smith 1993: 15–20)."</span>

<li id="cite_note-70"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-70">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBurger2008">Burger 2008</a>, pp.&#160;57–58: "<i>Poleis</i> continued to go to war with each other. The Peloponnesian War (431–404 BC) made this painfully clear. The war (really two wars punctuated by a peace) was a duel between Greece's two leading cities, Athens and Sparta. Most other <i>poleis</i>, however, got sucked into the conflict as allies of one side or the other&#160;... The fact that Greeks were willing to fight for their cities against other Greeks in conflicts like the Peloponnesian War showed the limits of the pull of Hellas compared with that of the polis."</span>

<li id="cite_note-71"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-71">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Fox, Robin Lane (2004). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.archaeology.org/online/interviews/fox.html">"Riding with Alexander"</a>. <i>Archaeology</i>. The Archaeological Institute of America. <q>Alexander inherited the idea of an invasion of the Persian Empire from his father Philip whose advance-force was already out in Asia in 336 BC. Philips campaign had the slogan of "freeing the Greeks" in Asia and "punishing the Persians" for their past sacrileges during their own invasion (a century and a half earlier) of Greece. No doubt, Philip wanted glory and plunder.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Archaeology&amp;rft.atitle=Riding+with+Alexander&amp;rft.date=2004&amp;rft.aulast=Fox&amp;rft.aufirst=Robin+Lane&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.archaeology.org%2Fonline%2Finterviews%2Ffox.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGreeks" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-149"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-149">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFCartledge2011">Cartledge 2011</a>, Chapter 4: Argos, p. 23: "The Late Bronze Age in Greece is also called conventionally 'Mycenaean', as we saw in the last chapter. But it might in principle have been called 'Argive', 'Achaean', or 'Danaan', since the three names that Homer does apply to Greeks collectively were 'Argives', 'Achaeans', and 'Danaans'."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-150"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-150">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFNagy2014">Nagy 2014</a>, Texts and Commentaries – Introduction #2: "Panhellenism is the least common denominator of ancient Greek civilization&#160;... The impulse of Panhellenism is already at work in Homeric and Hesiodic poetry. In the Iliad, the names "Achaeans" and "Danaans" and "Argives" are used synonymously in the sense of Panhellenes = "all Hellenes" = "all Greeks.""</span>

<li id="cite_note-Browning-159"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Browning_159-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBrowning1983">Browning 1983</a>, p.&#160;vii: "The Homeric poems were first written down in more or less their present form in the seventh century B.C. Since then Greek has enjoyed a continuous tradition down to the present day. Change there has certainly been. But there has been no break like that between Latin and Romance languages. Ancient Greek is not a foreign language to the Greek of today as Anglo-Saxon is to the modern Englishman. The only other language which enjoys comparable continuity of tradition is Chinese."</span>

<li id="cite_note-Harl-229"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Harl_229-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFHarl1996">Harl 1996</a>, p.&#160;260: "Cities employed the coins of an empire that formed a community of cities encircling the Mediterranean Sea, which Romans audaciously called "Our Sea" (<i>mare nostrum</i>).
